Blackie’s Modern Cyclopedia.
This is a handy encyclopedia in eight neat
volumes of workaday size. While not so
full or so up-to-date as the very expensive
publications of its class, it contains am
immense mass of varied information on
nearly all subjects of enquiry, and strikes a.
happy medium between the vast encyclopedia
in 20 or 30 heavy tomes and the disappoint-
ing single-volume coinpemlium.
Strongly-bound in cloth and well printed
the set of eight volumes for 505., packed or
posted anywhere in Australasia.

THE “KEEPER” PILE PILL
(Registered).

there are many remedies (some of them really beneficial)

For years the medicine had only a local and limited
use, but now arrangements have been completed to make
its merits more widely known, and the Australasian
colonies will have an opportunity of sharing with the old
country the benefits of this valuable remedy.
The medicine will be sold on its merits, in confident
assurance that its beneficent action will be a real boon to
sufferers.
It will not cure Piles in old and chronic cases by a single
dose. It will not cure every disease that flesh is heir to,
but it will cure Piles if taken in reasonable time, and
IT WILL GIVE RELIEF IN ALL CASES,
however stubborn and chronic, and the proprietors are
continually in receipt of most flattering and unsolicited
testimonials. Price, 2 6 per box (red label).

‘A GOLDEN SHANTY.’
Of all prisoners, writes an old N.S.W.
gaol-official, the Chinaman feels incarceration the
least. And which feels it most ? The hardy
ruffian who has exulted in the freedom of lawless-
ness and is now doomed to 20 years’ entombment?
The refined bank-swindler, who leaves a gay and
elegant society for the dismal solitude of the cell?
The love-sick bank-clerk, who has embezzled in
order to win the favor of the loved one’s eyes 7
The poor little sempstress, who has broken her
mother’s heart by stealing ribbon for a Sunday
dress? or the well-trained girl, nursed in a home
of purity and comfort, who has fallen fpom hope,
love and religion. No ! But there is one who
moans inside the gaol-wall, who is tortured by the
clank of chains, the snap of bolts, the click of
looks, the grating of warders’ boots upon the
sanded passages ; who repents not, yet the whole
time suffers agony ; who, conscienceless, is yet the
most afflicted ; who cannot sleep, or eat; whose
days are days of wretchedness, whose nights are
nights of woe. He is the child of Nature’s wilds
—the fast-disappearing aboriginal.
The Prince of Wales is just now starring
in the capacity of a heaven-born diplomatist. The
result of his visit to the dying Czar was a guar-
antee of everlasting friendship between Russia
and Great Britain. Albert Edward showed sur-
prising tact and artfulness at St. Petereburg, it
appears. No sooner had Death scratched the old
Czar’s name off the list of reigning monarchs than
H.R.H. saluted the new Czar with the title of
“Your Majesty.” He was positively the very
first person to clutch the opportunity, and
Nicholas 11. was deeply impressed. “0, uncle,”
cried the young man, when his grief had abated,
“ What a diplomatist you are, and what a pity
the English constitution precludes you from being
your country’s Minister for Foreign Affairs!”
Thus runs the story, which probably grew from a
small beginning of fact. Albert Edward can
scarcely be a less intelligent person than his
Russian nephew, and he has 25 years’ advantage
in theatrical and other experiences. Indeed, if
the peace of Europe, anyway, depended upon
young Nicholas knowing as much as his bald-
headed relative, Th# Bulletin would regard them
both with a favorable eye.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
Apply Sulpholine Lotion. It drives away
pimples, blotches, roughness, redness, and all dis-
figurements. Sulpholine developes a lovely skin.

Ed. Bulletin, —Let me tell you of the
“nearness” of some of the big monetary companies.
Nearly all of them have put their station-
managers on rations : flour, sugar, tea, salt, meat
and soap. In some cases the quantity of these
rations is limited to a certain weight, but where
this is done one ration is generously allowed for
visitors. On most Western stations, should the
manager rush into the wild extravagance of a tin
of jam or bottle of pickles it is forthwith debited
to his private account. I know one large station
where the manager had included 2 pkts. starch in
the list of supplies required, but the item was
struck out with an intimation that it was wholly
unnecessary. Another foolhardy manager ordered
2 gals, whisky among the stores for the
year, but this gave his directors such a
shock that he was promptly relieved of the
management. However, the fact that the co.’s
don’t consider that even a very limited quantity
of grog is necessary to the effective working of the
station doesn’t prevent their inspector partaking
very freely of the manager’s private supply when
he visits the station. I knew one back-country
station where potatoes were not deemed a neces-
sary. Well, the inspector arrived, and that night,
at dinner, was about to help himself from the
vegetable-dish when the storekeeper interposed :
“No you don’t! Those spuds are my private
property —bought ’em from the Chinkie last week.
The company don’t think ’em necessary for us—
and I don’t think ’em necessary for you.” That
storekeeper might have been seen next day riding
along a, lonely road to the nearest township withhis
swag in front of him ; however, he said he reckoned
he’d given the inspector a facer, and, after all, there
was more money in rabbiting. Yet another in-
stance of man-grinding. Mr. Blank had owned
a station for 17 years. The thing had been one
long pull at the devil’s tail. Blank was a first-
class manager, whose ability was recognised ; but
the station, which had originally been fairly good,
got over - run with pine scrub, necessitating
enormous outlay in clearing. Then came the
rabbits, and it had to be wire-netted at great
expense. Blank had been getting deeper and
deeper into the co., who had absorbed all his
original capital and all that the station had
returned, with the exception of .£3OO a year, which
had been allowed for private expenses. Then
the co. took possession of the place, but re-
tained him as manager at £2OO a year.
Shortly after this the co. took over another
station adjoining and placed it also under Blank’s
management, but while doubling his work and
responsibility they at the same time cut his screw
down to £l5O. Why did he stand it? Well, he
was a married man with a family, and he had to
stand it. Now you can understand that if the
co.’s are so disgustingly mean with their employes
who are in responsible positions the rank and file
are not likely to fare too well. Swiftly and surely
the whole of the Western Riverina country is
falling into the hands of big monetary institutions,
and where four men were employed a few years
ago one is now deemed enough, and if it were pos-
sible they would have that one at a wage that
would scarcely keep body and soul together. Now
we have the N.S.W. Legislative Council, which
may be said to largely represent these remorseless
institutions, endeavouring to emasculate the Land
Bill because they think some of its most useful
clauses inimical to the interests of the mortgagees.
—Kaito.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
•which you may pay by Post Office Order , One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
•whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But ive
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Slack and White,
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion — i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 9, 1895.
The Late Federal Conference.
The conference of Australian Premiers, at
Hobart, has thrown some more valuable
darkness on the vexed subject of Federation.
Considering the character of the assemblage,
this is about all that any reasonable person
expected it would throw. All the members
of the gathering were, it is true, sufficiently
enthusiastic about Federation in the abstract,
and among them they contributed a large
quantity of loose and eloquent sentiment to
the discussion, and pervaded the subject
with many magnificent perorations about the
glory and greatness and general haziness
of the coming Commonwealth. But, unfor-
tunately, everybody who deals with the sub-
ject contributes loose eloquence to the
Federal cause, and of that commodity there
is already a great deal too much. When the
conference settled down to details it proved
to be just as narrow, selfish, suspicious and
hopelessly divided as usual. The outcome
of its labors may be summed up in four
sentences: (1) Each member is willing co
federate if his own province gets its full
share, or a little more than its full share,
of financial advantage thereby ; but not
one, so far as appears at present, is
willing to sacrifice a copper for the
sake of unity. (2) Each one is willing
to federate if his own province gets its
full share, or a good deal more, of politi-
cal power. In this case the most ravenously
exorbitant of the crowd is Westralia, which
wants about fourteen times its fair propor-
tion of representation on a population basis;
and about the most moderate is New South
Wales, which only wants to run the show by
itself, or thereabouts. (3) Each province
demands that the Federal Parliament shall
be pledged to run the country for all time on
such lines as are required for the grinding of
its own unwholesome political axe. And (4)
the bosses of those provinces where the Fat
Man is uppermost demand that the Federal
constitution shall be so framed that Fat
Man shall remain uppermost in their own
territories, and that he shall get and remain
uppermost in the other provinces, and that
Uxiited Australia shall be run on solid Fat
principles for evermore. Apart from these
few points of disagreement the Conference
may be said to have been tolerably unani-
mous. /
« * *
Despite all differences of opinion, however,
certain useless “resolutions” were arrived
at by the vote of the majority, while an in-
dignant minority dissented violently in the
background. The decision of the majority
was that each province should pass an Act
authorising the election, by direct vote of
the people, of ten representatives to another
Federal Convention ; that the Convention
should draft a Federal Constitution ; and
that the Government of each province should
then submit the Constitution to a direct vote
of the people, by which vote it would either
be rejected or accepted without alteration.
This agreement, so far as it goes, is a simple,
equitable and business-like proposition, and,
but for certain conditions which make it null
and void and wholly useless, would offer a
better show of definite results than anything
yet suggested. But trouble began even at
this initial stage of the proceedings. Premier
Forrest, of Westralia, where the Fat Man
interest is paramount, demanded that the
members of the Convention shall be ap-
pointed, not by the people, but by Parlia-
ment, and that Parliament shall accept or
reject the Constitution without any popular
vote whatever. In Westralia, Parliament
is, to all intents and purposes, John
Forrest and his little group of Fat
supporters, and the kind of Federal
Constitution which would be acceptable
to this clique, and the kind which
would be accepted by a popular vote, are as
widely different as any two things can be.
Unless he gets his way, Westralia stands
out. Therefore Premier Forrest’s terms
virtually are that the Constitution of the
Commonwealth must be fixed to suit the
corrupt majority in the most corrupt and
least representative Legislature of the
smallest province (numerically speaking) in
Australia.
* * *
Premier Nelson, of Queensland, is another
dissentient on very similar grounds. Unlike
Forrest, Nelson does not demand that the
other provinces should decide the question
according to his own plan ; he only demands
complete freedom of action for himself. His
idea is that each provincial Government
should elect its ten representatives at the
Convention according to its own plan, and
should decide to accept or reject the Con-
stitution in any way it pleases. This
obviously means that, instead of the Queens-
land members being elected by the people
they will be appointed by the Boodlewraith
Cabinet, and instead of the Constitution being
submitted to the popular vote, it will be sub-
mitted to the Boodlewi’aith Parliament, the
other provinces, meanwhile, being at liberty
to conduct their own share of the business as
they please. This will ensure that the Fede-
ration scheme will be rejected if it gives the
Federal Parliament power to abolish cheap
nigger-labor and land-grant railways, or if it
provides for the election of that Parliament
by manhood suffrage instead of by a solid
property vote, or if it doesn’t provide an
Upper House of landholders, or otherwise
conform to the Fat Man standard. As the
sort of Constitution which the southern pro-
vinces would accept would never be adopted
by the Queensland Boodle Parliament, and
the one that Parliament would accept would
be rejected with loathing by the other
provinces, Queensland may also be counted
out for the present. When Nelson and
Forrest are both numbered with the poli-
tical dead the situation may be altered, but,
at present, the case is seemingly hopeless.
» • •
r Even apart from the two dissentient pro-
vinces, however, there is material enough to
go on with. A Commonwealth of N.S. W.,
Victoria, Tasmania, and S.A. would embrace
seven-eighths of the population and a still
larger proportion of the wealth of the coun-
try, and its weight and influence would
assuredly bring in the other two when their
present Fat Man bosses have gone to
their Parliamentary grave. But there are
other difficulties in the way. The conference
of Premiers adjourned in haste to permit
certain of its members to attend the sittings
of the Federal Council, and therefore many
of their interprovincial jealousies were sup-
pressed through want of time for expression.
For this, however, they made abundant
amends at the Council, h Barlow, an obscure
banker from Queensland, enthusiastically
denounced the proposal to submit anything
whatsoever to the popular suffrage, on the
brazen assumption that only “agitators”
would vote, and expressed a cordial hope
that the proposed Federal movement would
be a failure, and that the slow and impotent
Federal Council would remain the only
emblem of Australian “unity.” J. M.
Davies, of Victoria, objected to the “pool-
ing ” of the various public debts, on the
ground that it might mean each province
guaranteeing the liabilities of its neighbors.
This, undoubtedly, is just what Federation
will mean—in fact, it can’t possibly mean
anything else, and this objection to the
first essential of union is significant. The
same turbid authority objected to the estab-
lishment of a Federal Court of Appeal for
the stereotyped reason that “ the time is not
yet ripe.” Most people consider that the time
is not only ripe, but tolerably rotten,
and, in any case, it is difficult to see how there
can be a Federation without a Federal Court
of Appeal. The Council, however, decided
that, as a substitute for that institution, “the
Queen” should be requested to appoint an
Australian Judge to hear Australian appeals
in London, so that the local suitor will have
to go 12,000 miles at enormous expense to
get the same justice which the same Judge
could have given him in Australia. Also,
Australia is to pay the salary of this Judge—
who will get, no doubt, an extra allowance to
compensate for his unnecessary remoteness.
Tims the Council, even while talking Federa-
tion, was doing its best to make any real
Federation impossible.
# • *
Then Fysh, of Tasmania, announced that
his province would not, in any case, give up
the duty on Queensland sugar, so that
interprovincial Freetrade, which is another
necessary concomitant of Federation, ap-
parently goes by the board, if Fysh can
have it so. Arch hr, an ancient remnant
of the M‘llwraith regime, stated—what
everybody already knew-that Queensland’s
Boodle Cabinet would not federate unless
the Parliament of the Commonwealth was
prohibited from touching the Kanaka labor
question ; the cheap and murderous nigger
being of more importance, in the opinion of
Boodlewraith, than Australian unity. Hao
kett, a dull nonentity from Westralia,
declared that his Government would have
nothing whatever to do with any scheme
which referred anything to a popular vote.
The fallen Patterson, speaking on behalf of
the province which lately rejected him and
all his works, demanded that the Constitu-
tion Bill of the forgotten 1891 Convention
should be adopted. Dobson, of Tasmania,
suggested that New South Wales should be
ejected altogether from the Commonwealth.
Finally, there was a violent disagreement, in
the course of which the Victorian members
withdrew altogether from the meeting, and
the Federal Council closed up its abortive
existence for the year.
t « •
Towards the close of the alleged proceed-
ings, however, Premier Turner contrived
to cast an entirely new gloom over the pro-
spects of the Commonwealth. According to
the scheme of the Conference, the various
Legislatures are to pass a bill authorising the
people of each province to elect ten repre-
sentatives to a Convention ; the Convention
is to draft a Federal Constitution ; the Con-
stitution is to be submitted to a popular vote
for acceptance or rejection ; and if accepted
the Parliaments are to address the Imperial
Legislature asking for the necessary Enabling
Act. The bill authorising this procedure
will probably be passed without very much
difficulty in New South Wales and South
Australia, but the fat and aggressive Legis-
lative Councils of Victoria and Tasmania,
with their inborn hatred of an appeal to the
people in any form, are likely to block the
way. Assuming, however, that the measure
is passed, that the people elect Democratic
representatives, that these representatives
sink their local jealousies sufficiently to
frame a Democratic Constitution which will
meet all requirements, and that the popular
vote accepts it by a huge majority—even
then, so Turner carefully explains, nothing
whatever will have been done. The popular
vote won’t count for anything at all. The
Constitution must still go through both
Houses of the Legislature in the good old
way, so that if it is Democratic enough to
pass the popular vote it will be rejected, or
amended out of all shape and form, by the
bigoted Tories of the Upper House; and if it
is bigoted enough to satisfy the Upper
House the people will have none of it.
Moreover, even the manner of appointing
the delegates to the Convention is—despite
the resolution to the contrary—to be left to
the option of Parliament, so that there may be
no referendum at all, and the delegates may
be appointed by Parliament, or by the
Ministry, or by the Upper House, or by a
constituency of property-holders, if the Fat
Party can contrive to have it so. Therefore,
the popular vote might as well be left out
of the scheme in the first instance, for, even
if it is authorised by the Legislature, and
taken in due form, it means nothing.
Federation is back at the same old deadlock
as before, and nothing has been accom-
plished, and nothing seems any more likely
to be accomplished than hitherto. A certain
amount of flabby eloquence has been added
to the previous accumulation, but otherwise
things are as they were.
From Gutter to Gallows.
Grenon was a dog with a bad name. Grenon is
hanged duly. He was very possibly innocent of
the particular crime for which he was executed;
but that is a trifle. Chief Justice Barley
comforted him with the reflection that he was
bound to get to the scaffold sooner or later ;
and as he was only a poor devil of a French
convict—friendless, without influence, without
respectable connections, his brutality unre-
deemed by any suggestion of pathos—society
easily salves the thing it calls its conscience.
There is no scarcity of French convicts:
one more or less makes little difference.
Besides, if Grenon did not shoot Heavey, no
doubt he had done lots of things just as bad ;
and, if he had got the chance, he might do many
things worse. Better hang him on general
principles. And as he was such a fool as to
noose his own neck . . . let him take the
consequences of his folly. Give these degraded
brutes to understand, once for all, that if they
choose to be ignorant of our language and our
forms of law, there is for them neither hope nor
mercy. Tell them that the majestic engine which
we call Justice cannot pause in its course because
some suspicious Ishmaelite recklessly refuses
proffered aid, and flings prudence to the winds
in order to asseverate what appear to his
deranged wits conclusive proofs of innocence.
He should learn to know better. What business
had he to be born in a gutter and educated on
the hulks ? Hang him ! hang him ! and inform
the bourgeois god of the collection-plate that,
society having ruined his body, Heaven is wel*
come to his soul.
• 9 9
Guenon must have shot Heavey ; because
Heavey says so. There’s no one to corroborate
Heavey ; but that proves nothing. Grenon,
the consensus of evidence shows, could only
speak broken English, yet he sustained
a fluent conversation with Heavey for a
full quarter of an hour, Heavey says so. This
of itself proves that Grenon shot Heavey.
Besides, Heavey saw Grenon plainly in a
dimly-lighted street at one o’clock in the morn-
ing. Heavey says so. Anyhow, somebody
shot Heavey, and if it was not Grenon, who
was it ? No more suitable person could be
found to shoot Heavey than Grenon, the well-
known criminal. Everybody knows that is just
the thing Grenon would do. Therefore,
Grenon must have shot Heavey. Heavey could
not have shot himself by accident if his assailant
rushed upon him and grasped his wrist,
because the bullet has been found to weigh
slightly less than Heavey’s bullets. It is true
that a bullet loses something in weight on
firing, and something more on passage through
a body; but that proves nothing. It is true
that Heavey’s cartridges and Grenon’s cart-
ridges were of the same calibre, and would fit
either revolver; but that proves nothing.
Besides, Heavey says he didn’t shoot himself;
and that is conclusive. Heavey was a
nonentity ; now he is a hero ; and no man who
shot himself by accident could be a hero.
Therefore, Heavey didn’t shoot himself.
Therefore, Grenon shot Heavey. No other
person could have shot Heavey— not the
Frenchman, for example, who helped Grenon
to buy his revolver, and who did the English
talking to the shopman. E’er the police have
failed to find another Frenchman ; therefore,
there was no other Frenchman; therefore, he
could not have shot Heavey. Besides, Heavey
says Grenon shot him. Therefore, it was im
possible for Grenon to have gone a-burgling
with a mate who shot Heavey and, knowing
the locality, escaped ; while Grenon ran into a
cul-de-sac and was caught.
* * *
For, if Grenon did not shoot Heavey, why
was he caught ? The man who shot Heavey
had to be caught; Grenon was caught; there-
fore Grenon shot Heavey. Besides, Constable
Long, the gallant officer who caught Grenon
with the help of three tramway employes
(and was promoted next day), is sure Grenon
would have shot him also if he could ; there-
fore, Grenon must have shot Heavey. It is
true that when Constable Long caught Grenon,
Grenon’s revolver was loaded in each of the
six chambers, while he only bought six car-
tridges with the revolver. It is true that G.
B. Modini, a Sydney expert with a life time’s
experience in fire-arms, asserts that Grenon’s
revolver had never been fired, because the
grease in the cartridge-chambers and in
the barrel was intact, while there was no
smell or mark of explosion whatever ; and any
man with a new nickel-plated revolver may
try, if he chooses, to fire a cartridge without
destroying the grease, and without leaving a
mark—which would grow more obvious with
time. It is true that Mr. Mclntosh, another
expert in fire-arms, and the man who sold a
revolver supposed to have been Grenon’s, also
says that to the best of his knowledge and
belief Grenon’s revolver had not been fired,
giving the same reasons as Mr. Modini. But
these things prove nothing. Constable Long
declares that one chamber of the revolver had
undoubtedly been fired and re-loaded, because
he smelt the smell of discharge. Two other
constables also smelt the smell, and say the
same thing. No one who is familiar with the
marvellous exactitude and perspicacity which are
the jewels in the dower of every policeman, can
for a moment question the constables’ state-
ments. Therefore (from the constabulary point
of view) Modini is a liar; Mclntosh is a
fool; and Grenon shot Heavey.
* « •
But if Grenon re-loaded his revolver, lie
must have had fresh cartridges, and he is only
known to have bought six. This difficulty
also disappears. For, fortunately enough,
Constable Long, wandering over the scene of his
heroic struggle, says he picked up several cart-
ridges (320 bore ; the commonest of all; sold in
bushels by every gun-shop in town) which were
the same as Grenon’s cartridges, and which
plainly were thrown away by him in order to
avert suspicion, when he felt himself beaten by
Constable Long and the tramway employes.
Therefore, Grenon must have shot Heavey.
If he didn’t shoot Heavey, why did he carry a
revolver ? He answers by spinning a cock-and-
bull yarn to the effect that he was frightened
of some men at Parramatta—a yarn which only
a lunatic would expect to be believed. Singu-
larly enough, Dr. Violette, surgeon to Parra-
matta gaol, says that on two occasions,
in two separate years, Grenon teas a lunatic,
or, at any rate, showed undoubted signs of
mental derangement; and, still more singu-
larly, each of these periods of derangement
occurred at about the same time of the year—
in September or October—at which Grenon
shot Heavey. But this proves nothing, for
what is there to show that Grenon was
deranged last year ? Only his behaviour before
the trial; at the trial—when he threw away his
life by refusing to call the evidence above sum-
marised ; and subsequently to the trial, when
he spent his time in making dough images,
“ obviously to create the impression that he
was deranged,” says the sapient reporter. But
Chief Justice Darley did not see his way to
protect the wretched man from himself ;
though expressly permitted by the Criminal
Law Amendment Act to re-open the case in


order to hear material evidence—like that
of Mr. Modini, Mr. Mclntosh, and Dr.
Yiolette. For, as Chief Justice Dabley said,
Grenon was bound for the gallows, anyhow.
So, no doubt, it was not worth while bothering.
The sooner he reached his goal the better.
There’s nothing in the fact that Heavey was
only wounded, not killed ; nothing in the argu-
ment that even under the savage Jewish code
life was only taken in exchange for life ; nothing
in the statement that the law which, in New
South Wales, hangs for an alleged attempt to
kill, with the intention unproven, is part of the
dregs of convict discipline, and is abolished in
England and in every other civilised country.
Even if innocent, a man like Grenon deserves
to be hanged for having a bad reputation. What
right, asked the Executive Council, eager to
get off to its junketing in Tasmania and else-
where— what right has a man to have a bad
reputation?
* • •
Though The Bulletin does not consider
capital punishment justifiable under any cir-
cumstances, it would like to be able always to
intellectually respect the decisions of the
Supreme Court and the Executive. The deci-
sion to hang Grenon this paper regards, in face
of the grave doubts disclosed by the evidence,
as a blasphemous usurpation, by a set of very
dull and commonplace people, of the omniscience
of Almighty God.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Touching Tariffs.
The Vic. Tariff Commission listened last week
to the bitter cry of a British hat-importer. He
testified, not as one suffering from the effects of
local production, but as a philanthropist
anxious to abolish “second tariffs” and assist
the public. Lower duties would mean lower
wages for Melbourne hat-makers, thanks to the
blessed competition of cheap labor in England,
and thus the community (outside of the hat-
trade and its family connections) would be
benefited. It is the privilege of every importer
to have the public welfare at heart, assuming
the public to consist solely of other importers
and capitalists living on their means. The dis-
tinction between the public that this importer
speaks of and Victoria’s inhabitants in general
is very broad. If a reduction of hat duties had
no worse effect than a fall in wages at
the Melbourne factories, the country would
even then, according to its Legislative Council
doctrines, lose in money “circulation.” The hat-
makers, like a retrenched Governor, would be
deprived of so much aggregate salary for
“ spending in the colony,” and if it is good to
give His Excellency an extra =£3ooo to expend
in ball-room festivities it must be good to pro-
vide humble toilers with the means of purchas-
ing a few more household necessaries. But if
lower duties, in addition to reducing wages,
reduce local production and divert Victorian
money to English pockets, the country must
lose coin as well as “circulation.” Naturally,
the real object of the hat-importer who offered
his views to the Tariif Commission, is to benefit
the “public” at the expense of the country.
The duties he pleads for would permit of English
hats re-entering the Melbourne market, if not
as freely as of yore, i n sufficiently large
consignments to seriously affect the local mills.
In short, this importer, and every other de-
scription of importer, hankers after a Revenue
Tariff calculated to swell the Customs receipts
from goods that have to be paid for abroad
with Victorian money. It is an aimless farce,
this taking of evidence concerning well-estab-
lished industries. The witnesses interested in
foreign trade are unanimously in favor of a
Revenue Tariff, whereas the local manufac-
turers and traders who are independent of
Flinders Lane desire a prohibitive tariff on
goods that can be made in Victoria. After
hearing both sides, the Commission finds itself
left with nothing save the Protection principle
to consider. As regards goods which have no
immediate, or even possible, bearing upon
Victoria’s settled policy, the Radical members
of the Commission will advise a popular tariff,
and the Tories will advocate the blessed
Revenue Tariff, seeing that indirect taxation of
the masses prevents direct taxation of the
classes.
Deakin’s Apostacy.
Leakin', the alleged Democrat, was one of those
who voted at the Federal Council for the ap-
pointment of an Australian Judge to hear Aus-
tralian appeals on the Judicial Committee of
the British Privy Council. Deakin is an Aus-
tralian native, and has always posed as an ad-
vanced Liberal and an exponent of true Aus-
tralian sentiment. He has never done anything
of a definite character to help the Democratic
programme in Australia, but in a loose, vague,
unspecified fashion he has passed as one of the
main supports of the progressive movement.
By his action in the present instance, however,
Deakin has proved himself a fraud, a delusion,
and a commonplace lawyer. Australia doesn’t
want to send a Judge to England to administer
justice which he could supply of exactly the
same quality on the spot. It is dead tired of
the system by which the rich litigant may
ruin the poor one by dragging him to the other
end of the world, and putting him to several
years’ delay, and making him incur an enor-
mous outlay in travelling and law ex.’s, before
ho can get his case finally settled. Deakin
and his fellow delegates knew well enough
that the Legislative Assemblies of, at least,
three of the provinces would have thrown out
the corrupt and ridiculous proposal with scorn
if it had been brought before them, so they
arranged that the irresponsible Federal Council
should address the Queen on the subject, and
endeavour to work the lawyers’ job with-
out the people’s representatives having a voice
in the matter. Then, when the appointment
is made, and the salary of the appointee
is included in the Budget, the various
Parliaments will be informed, according
to the good old phrase, that it would
be “ ungracious” to refuse to pay. The Bul-
letin devoutly hopes that the people’s repre-
sentatives will be “ungracious” in this case;
every member who isn’t should be unhesita-
tingly rejected by the Democracy forever after-
wards. If Deakin and co. found it absolutely
necessary to address the Queen in a grovelsome
strain on this particular matter, why didn’t
they beseech her to establish a final Court of
Appeal in Australia, with jurisdiction over
Australia, Tasmania, Maoriland, Fiji and
British New Guinea, and send out or otherwise
appoint two Judges to boss the institution?
Then Australia might have paid their salaries
with the satisfaction of knowing that it was
receiving reasonable value for its money by
getting its justice more promptly and more
cheaply than heretofore. This scheme would
have contained all the necessary elements of
grovel, and would also have been practical,
business-like and economical; the other hasn’t
a single feature to recommend it to any intelli-
gent community.
In the Multitude of Councillors !
The Hawthorn (Vic.) Council last week dis-
cussed the minimum wage, and in the name of
Supply and Demand refused relief to some of
its wretched constituents who are struggling in
the deep of 3s. 6d. a day for a man’s wage :
Cr. Edgerton admitted that the 6s. 2d. paid in Haw-
thorn for tlie hire of a man, horse and dray was less than
a living wage, hut held that the law of supply and demand
must always regulate the price of labor.
Cr. D alley ; You forget that horse-feed is ever so much
cheaper now.
Cr. Edgerton: Yes, but the drivers get only 3s. 6d. a
day for themselves.
Cr. Smith : All the more reason for adopting the mini-
mum wage.
The Mayor held that low wages tended to lower the
community both socially and morally.
Cr. Ardagh said that since the council began paying
the 6s. 2d. per day for horse work the owners of horses
and drays found it impossible to get more from outsiders.
The harm done by this one act of the council was incal-
culable. In fact, men with horsesand drays were working
for 18s. a week, which hardly covered the cost of horse-
feed and repairs.
The motion for the minimum wage was then put and
rejected, only the Mayor, Cr. Barbour and Crs. Smith
and ardagh voting for it.
All honor is due to the tiny minority that voted
on the side of humanity on this occasion, but it
is almost a tempting prospect that 40 more
councils may rise up and call the minimum
wage cursed. Then the community will rise,
inspired, and enact one-man-one-vote for muni-
cipalities. The consequence for the swarming
miserables on the councils will be everlasting
erasure from public life, and from the pickings
of municipal office which they provide for their
own fattening. In Victoria the municipal
bodies have almost all proved arrant traitors to
the cause of Democracy. Every man with a
vote can put one nail in the coffin of the worst
form of treason.
Prorogued.
Another session of the Vic. Parliament has
been concluded in a violent hurry, and some
sadly farcical incidents marked its close. The
Legislative Assembly sat up, or lay down and
snored, through hot nights, for the purpose of
accomplishing reforms and economies which
were presently repudiated by the Legislative
Council, so that when the curtain fell upon the
performance of public business the tax-
payers were left lamenting a great waste of
applause. They had been deluded by phantoms
and cheated into admiring deeds that remained
undone. One very late and sultry sitting of the
Assembly was mainly devoted to a visionary re-
duction of the Agent-General’s salary. The salary
was cut down to =£lsoo a year, also the House
decided to inaugurate this very urgent reform
in the case of Duncan Gillies, a person who
receives several hundreds of pounds more than
any other Australian Agent-General for being
far and away the laziest member of the
fraternity. The retrenchment of Gillies
appeared to happen at about 3.30 a.m. A few
hours later the Council negatived this piece of
admirable legislation in the curtest manner on
record. President Zeal, an old friend of
Gillies aforesaid, failed to hear somebody call
for a division, and declared that the bill had
died without a struggle. It was practically a
money bill, but it was passed by the people’s
representatives only to pass away in “ another
place,” as the Land-tax had disappeared a fort-
night earlier. The Assembly decided to abolish
primage duties, in the interests of business
convenience and the poor consumer. The
Council quietly reimposed those duties at the
suggestion of Matthew Davies’s brother. “If
they were repealed there might be a deficiency
at the end of the year, and a consequent
excuse for bringing in another Land-tax,” said
J. M. Davies. He spoke transparent nonsense,
inasmuch as the primage duties were to remain
in force anyway until the last month of the
financial year, but the Council turned pale at
the mention of “ land-tax,” and another chicken
hatched off the Assembly’s all-night sittings
got its little neck wrung. A bill to prevent
the absurd lapsing of measures between sessions,
whereby much time and talk is annually
wasted, had been carried in the “Lower”
House. This, likewise, was dishonored by the
48 landowners, for the reason that a measure
affecting Proputty might be carried forward to
next half-year, and the Council would prefer
any such iniquity to lapse in the good old
manner. The first session of the new Victorian
Parliament began with a democratic roar and
degenerated into a Tory chuckle.
♦-
As further details come to hand the stor, --
the Newfoundland banking crisis grows more
interesting. Newfoundland is, at the best of
times, a poor, miserable little place with a
population of 200,000 souls, very few roads, an
interior region that is uninhabited and almost
wholly inaccessible, and with hardly any means
of support save fish. It is estimated that all
the cash in the province before the,crisis didn’t
exceed .£33,000, and of this over .£BO,OOO was
locked up in the fallen banks. When the
smash came everything went down with a run.
Two-thirds of the shops closed straight away,
and sacked all their employes. Nearly all the
hh-curiug and fish-dealing establishments did
Lkewise. The seal-fishery, one of the main
supports of the place, broke down at once
because the owners' of the seal-fishing fleet
were bankrupt and could neither pay for
stores nor get them on credit. The cod-
fishery, which is the other prop and stay of
the foggy island, is expected to do likewise for
the same reason. On a rough estimate, three-
fourths of the inhabitants were out of work
when the mail left. The church funds are mostly
gone in the broken banks, and even the means
of grace are closed for repairs. At the principal
orphanage in St. John’s the children were lately
leading a precarious existence on one meal a
day, because the banks have got away with the
orphanage funds also. The whole cash assets
of the Treasury—which weren’t much in any
case—are locked up, and in all probability will
never be heard of again ; the taxes have stopped
coming in owing to the general paralysis ; the
Ministry has resigned ; the hungry mob has
taken to smashing things as a protest against
the doings of the local Fat Man; and everything
is chaos. A postscript to the report sets forth
that the trouble arose through the “ depression
in silver.” Considering that all the specie to
be depressed amounted to the aforesaid
£85,000, the explanation looks improbable.
“ Depression in commercial morality” is amuck
more probable explanation.

Q. Attorney-General Byrnes, at the Federal
Conference, carried a motion that Queen Yic. be
asked to call an Australian Judge to sit on the
Privy Council. Another legal hound on the trail !
But it seems quaint that at a gathering pro-
fessedly held in the interests of Australian.
Federation—which inevitably means thecreation
of a supreme Australian judiciary, whose deci-
sions in Australian cases will be final—even a
lawyer should have the brass to advocate
sending an Australian Judge to England to
decide those cases. What do the lawyers
want, anyhow ? Do they want justice for
Australian suitors ? or do they want to job a
big lawyer into a toney position at ’Ome, with a
big salary (to be a fresh charge on the tax-
payers), leaving more room for the small fry to
fight for the jobs out here ? Everybody knows
the brutal truth is they want the job, and don’t
care 3s. 4d. about the justice. For, if the
essential element in Privy Council justice is to
be the Australian Judge, isn’t the Judge’s
justice quite as good here as 16,000 miles
away? And what is the use of charging suitors
to take their cases to England, when they can
have exactly the same brand of law here at
25 per cent, less cost ? And if the essential
thing about the matter is the job, are the
Australian Parliaments going to permit it ?

S.M. Herald has been asked to correct the
unfortunate admission that the new gag is
meant as a punishment. The use of the imple-
ment cannot possibly be attended with cruelty,
says Minister Gould ; and it is not meant to
punish, only to silence. These official dis-
claimers avail nothing. A man cannot be made
dumb by forcibly closing or distending his jaws
with a metal instrument without cruelty ; and,
if the instrument be applied in private by a
brutal or angry warder, the cruelty is likely to
be very great. Red-tape safeguards will be as
ineffective in the future as they have been in
the past. The use of the gag was abolished in
New South Wales in consequence of the
horrors reported by a special judicial com-
mission to attend its use—not its abuse.
For the use of the gag must always be abuse.
The form of it matters little ; the character of
the men who employ it matters much; and that
remains the same as ever it was. The point is
not that the new gag is an innocent instrument;
it is that in the hands of gaol officials the
most innocent instrument—authority for its use
once given—can be, and habitually is, con-
verted into an instrument of devilish torture.
The Herald, as the official mouthpiece, says
that the existence, not necessarily the use, of
the gag is absolutely necessary. This implies
that prisoners’ knowledge of what awaits
them will act as a deterrent. If the gag is
harmless, how will it deter ? In the opinion
of the authorities, the class of prisoners
whom it is desired to gag do not stick at
trifles, or it would not be necessary to gag
them. There is thus an inadvertent official
admission that the new gag is no trifle, but a bit
of grim earnest ; since even the fear of it may
be generally relied on to tame the stoutest
criminal. And if the dread of the gag is
enough to silence most prisoners, what must the
gag itself be like ?
That Kensington (Sydney) licensing case
shows one of the weak points of local
option. Residents of the semi - genteel
suburb, or, at least, a large proportion
of them, opposed by petition an application
for a new (in fact, the first) license. The
magistrate granted the license, and explained
that as the locality is now much frequented,
especially on account of the racecourse recently
opened, the travelling public and visitors have
to be studied as well as the residents. It
is evident that the hotel is for the conveni-
ence of visitors rather than of residents.
The resident can fall back on a well-stocked
cellar or a modest cupboard, whereas the
stranger is dependent on the hotel. Therefore,
it is unfair that the strangers’ accommodation
should be dependent on the residents’ votes.
The residents can shut up the hotel and get all
they want through the grocer or the spirit
dealer. The local option vote of a suburb does
not profess to stop the grocer’s cart from the
centre of the city. And if the local option vote
of a city ward stopped the grocer’s license, the
numerous suburban boozers whose “ reasonable
requirements ” were supplied by his cart,
would have no say whatever in a matter so
deeply concerning them. In more than one
sense, local option is a too narrow measure.
There will evidently have to be also local option
in (amongst other things) racecourses.
English Admiral Oolomb recently wrote in the
National Review that in war-time the agree-
ment which binds the “ Australian ” squadron
to remain in Australian waters would be so
much waste paper. Admiral Bridge, who has
succeeded to the command of the squadron,
said practically the same thing to a S.M.
Herald interviewer. Only a nervous Admiral,
he implied, would be bound by the agreement:
a strong one would defy it. Now, this obliga-
tion on the part of the squadron to remain
in Australian waters is the sole remnant of
authority which we can show over the vessels
which we maintain. Iti or but of Australian
waters the Admiral whosb Salary we pay is a
law to himself, and does as he pleases irrespec-
tive of the bidding of parliaments or peoples.
In the direction of'thfe fleet which we are taxed
to support, in the choice of its officers and sea-
men, we have mo voice whatever. The only
thing to show that the squadron is “Austra-
lian ” at all is The obligation to rettiain ih
Australian waters. And now two English
Admirals tell us That The agreement will be
broken ait discretion, and that “our ships”
may meander any where from the Mediterranean
to the Gulf of Mexico, without the slightest
.attention being paid to Australian protests.
Should England be at war, and the Channel
fleet or the China fleet be endangered, we may
whisfcle for our squadron. The Naval Defence
Act was indeed a triumph of Australian
cdiplomacy !
The Argus of to-day does not hastbn slbwly
like the Argus of the olden time, which cer-
tainly possessed a rag of dignity, tn the lead-
ing article of the 21st January it ‘roared like
this :
The Melbourne of to-day would hftve beeh the greatest
port in Australasia had it not been sacrificed to the im-
poverishing policy of establishing manufactures which
Ra\e ever been wealth consumers instead Pf wealth pro-
ducers. Every step we took downwards placed Sydney
two steps above us, until to-day We see that city tha
mistress of these seas, conducting the greater part of the
transhipment trade which was almost Wholly oUrs thirty
years ago. J -
But the commercial ‘editor put in a. quiet correc-
tion in his column the very next day. He
quoted the official figures to show that, despite
the bad times and the falling prices, the re-
exports of foreign merchandise had only fallen
from <£1,695,915 in ’B9 to .£1,567,727 in ’93.
the transhipments also had suffered in the same
better-than-expected manner. The commercial
editor arrives at the following conclusions
(l)That the transhipment trade to other colonies has
not suffered more than direct exports from abroad to
those colonies, and (2nd) that the orders placed with
Melbourne merchants from other colonies have fallen off
to a moderate extent in value, but not necessarily in bulk
owing to the fail in prices. ’
This forms a splendid reply to 'the bunyip-roar
of the leading columns. “ The attempt to beat
up a Freetrade rally by exciting a parochial
rivalry between Sydney and Melbourne indi-
cates the dotage of journalism, and the desperate
condition of the official Argus could not better
be typified. The port regulations and condi-
tions of both Melbourne and Sydney need
amendment; but the two ports should not and
will not be conducted on the pitiable methods
to which some modern journals have reduced
themselves.

Witness after witness appeared before the Yic.
iariff Board the other day to describe how the
clothing industry was no more than an oppor-
tunity for sweating—and the representatives of
importing houses declared in succession that the
protective tariff increased the evil. The fact,
however, is that tailors and tailoresses have
always and everywhere been a disunited and
weak-minded body, and the competition between
the sexes has been perhaps one of the chief factors
in the demoralisation of this class of workers. Of
late years the demoralisation has been rendered
more complete by the increased factory
provision for the employment of girl-labor at
nominal wages. Clothing manufacturers are,
as a class, the most heartless in the abuse of
human labor, and no fiscal system, whether
Freetrade or Protective, will change their
rapacity into decency. Protection can indeed
offer the localisation of an industry to the
worker. But, in a competitive system where
the worker’s helplessness is the" employer’s
opportunity, no other result can be expected but
the exploitation of the worker. The irresistible
conclusion is that the community must not only
ensure proper employment, but proper remu-
neration as well, and the establishment of a
minimum wage for protected industries is a
reasonable suggestion for establishing a large
body of workers on a humane basis. Protected
industries were established by the community
for a set purpose—namely, the advantage of
the community, and any policy or attituefe on
the part of protected manufacturers antagonis-
tic to the commonweal should be immediately
checked by enactment. Sweating may be a
puzzle, but the Minimum Wage is the answer.
Dear Bulletin, — Re parsons in politics, put
there by the Labor leagues. You describe
Smailes, M.P., ex-parson, as “the ghastliest
failure in the present New South Wales As-
sembly.” The system under which the ex-
parson was selected is also a ghastly failure.
Mr. Smailes joined Granville L.E.L. about six
weeks before the selection of candidates. Before
that, he was totally unknown in Granville,
being an elector and resident of Parramatta.
He joined the League, to use his own words,
“at the request of six of the best men in the
Granville L.E.L.” Two out of the six were
not members of the League ; the other four
were people whose vote was. the only
thing of value they possessed. The secre-
tary of the League was immediately inundated
with applications for membership by electors of
Parramatta, who, because there was no Labor
League in that town, were, according to our
rules (since altered), eligible to become mem-
bers of the Granville League and assist in the
selection of a candidate for whom at an election
they could not vote. Also, all the members of
Smailes’ congregation residing in Granville, old
and young, pursued the secretary night
and day to have their names put on
the League books. The League being thus
stuffed, Smailes’ calling and election were
sure ; and after his selection the majority of
those who joined for the express purpose of
voting for him never turned up at a meet-
ing. He was returned to Parliament, and
he owes his return to the unwearied exertions
of those who opposed his selection by the
League, but, after his selection, loyally voted
for him. The Granville League subscribed ,£l6
to pay Mr. Smailes’ election expenses; yet from
then till now he has never attended a meeting
of the League. He was requested to deliver a
lecture in Granville in aid of the widow of the
late fireman, Brown, and replied that he would
deliver an open-air sermon. His offer was
declined.— Edmond Dantes, Granville, 21/1/95.

SUNDRY SHOWS.

“Sowing the Wind,” at Sydney Lyceum,
did excellent business all through its first week,
and at time of writing is starting on its second lap
with much enthusiasm. There is a pleasant old-
world flavor about this production which is re-
freshing to the eye, and restful to the weary little
mass of grey thinking matter with which man
worries through this world of care. Virtue in a
sea-green suit, and with a high concern like a
tablecloth wrapped round its throat, is a plea-
santer thing to look at than Virtue in a murky
black coat and an oppressive collar. Niss Noble’s
cackling maiden, whose titter is a work of art and
the finished expression of girly foolishness, looks
more appropriate in her white stockings and in-
visible shoes, and with her waist hauled right up
to her ears, than she could do in any modern
costume. Also, the elaborate, old - fashioned
courtesy—in these days, it seems, a lady courte-
sied separately to each person in the room,
and each courtesy occupied fully 60 seconds
by the watch—is remindful of a period when
people had time for snch things. Nowadays, a
bank could reconstruct, and convert half of its
deposits into preference shares, in the time occu-
pied by that one soulful ceremony. Brough’s
sour, meddlesome patriarch grows upon one with
longer acquaintance, and the moral that there is
no ass like the moral old ass with good intentions,
gains extra force every night. Also, Mrs. Brough,
in the part of the good woman who was brought
up by a lost mother and a hairless peer of the
deepest dye, and yet preserved her character un-
stained, is a noble improbability which this clever
lady makes so natural that no one really cares
whether it is probable or not. Upon further con-
sideration, The Bulletin is inclined to think that
the average audience doesn’t care much whether
any artistic character is probable or not. And,
upon still further consideration, this paper is in-
clined to think that nobody cares much about the
probability of anything.
** * *
The water-pantomime at Woodloch’s circus
proved an eminently acceptable show, and will be
repeated nightly until further notice. Over 100
performers are engaged in this entertainment,
which is suggestive of a comic insurrection in
Sidney harbour, combined with a Chinese inva-
sion and various other circumstances. The
equestrian part of the show still remains on the
programme, so that Woodloch furnishes a large
and massive entertainment at a very moderate
cost.
» • •
At the above show, on Saturday, the
rains fell upon the big tent, and turned its outside
wqdls into miniature Niagaras; and the winds
blew and beat upon that tent and whisked away a
goodly strip of its roof, gut the gap was covered,
a successful matinee held in the afternoon, and a
good crowd pleased in the evening. There are 40
male and female performers, 32 performing horses,
many wise and wondrous dogs, and monkeys whose
intelligence is such that the youngest gentleman
of them, when offered a glass of water, looks as
hurt as a Brisbane drummer would.
** * *
About the latter part of this week the
wildly-gifted James MacMahon reopens the pre-
mises at 148 Pitt-street, Sydney—formerly occu-
pied by the late lamented kinetoscope—with a
new illusion called the Haunted Swing. The
Bulletin doesn’t propose to give away the secret
of this mechanical contrivance, which the public
may, or may not, discover by experiment. Suf-
fice it to say that the visitor, upon payment of
the usual cash consideration at the door, is admit-
ted into a room and seated along with various
others, in a capacious vessel slung in the
usual fashion. Then he meets with one of the
most sudden and surprising circumstances which
ever happened to him in the whole course of his
chequered career, and he wants to know, in wild
amazement, where he is going to and what is going
to happen next. After that he departs a wiser
and more surprised man, and goes round the
corner to think it out.
** * *
Bland Holt’s “ Span of Life ” is still doing
its “last nights’’ in a manner which ought to
prove highly profitable to the management. The
Living Bridge shows no signs of decay, though it
has nightly to support the burden of Miss Hilda
Spong’s generous figure, with that of her stage
che-eild thrown in. On one evening, by the way,
either the lady’s nerve failed her as she crossed
the vasty chasm, or else the Living Bridge was
more bumpy than usual, for Miss Spong stopped
short in the middle of the crossing, and stood for
a considerable time with all her weight on the
centre mountebank. The fact that this trodden-
on person didn’t give by the breadth of a hair
under this sudden trial, shows that the bridge
waj3 constructed by a reliable contractor and is
thoroughly trustworthy.

Lord Yarmouth’s charity entertainment,
at Melb. Bijou, tasted until Saturday, by which
time he had got down to ordinary prices and
played himself out. The profits from his three
perfonnanses were considerable, no doubt, but the
young man might have turned his notoriety and
precious title to more account by raising a sub-
scription among the class of people who patronised
his show. One way and another, time, trouble
and money were wasted in feeding Yarmouth’s
vanity. A Government which prohibits, on pub-
lic grounds, an art union for a charitable purpose,
certainly ought to step in and prevent this outrage
on Art the next time it is attempted. The Bul-
letin says this after due consideration of the
Yarmouth matter in ail its bearings, public and
private, theatrical and social.
*• * * *
Yarmouth proved to be such a very bad
amateur actor that many people were deceived
into thinking him a passable dancer. He can't
dance at all, in the professional sense of the word.
He is nimble and light on his feet. The Earl’s
comparative proficiency at stage hopping leads
him into extravagances that are unwholesome as
well as ridiculous. He appears as a moth fooling
around graceful Miss Sadie Green, supposed to be
a candle. Gauze was hanging from his shoulders
and arms, and his general appearance suggested a
scraggy, hard-featured woman disguised as a pan-
tomime prince. He flitted after the candle on one
leg, practising a plain swimming stroke the while.
A flash of light at the wings as he made his
exit in pursuit of Miss Green signified that the
moth had met his fiery doom. Lord Yarmouth
took his part in all seriousness, and with a pained
expression of countenance. He didn’t seem to
feel his position even when he impersonated a
comic miller, in fancy costume, and sang “Knocked
’em in the Old Kent Road ” as though it were a
ballad of high life, instead of a coster ditty.
Imagine a long, thin chappie with Charles 11. curls,
white smalls and doublet, silver-trimmed cloak to
match—imagine this spook chortling in a minor
key, “Thought I should, haw! died.” Our
colonial oath ! the event might really have come
to pass had Harry Rickards been present. And
the nobleman was doing this sort of thing for the
benefit of distressed actors.
* # #
Lord Yarmouth’s “ serpentine dance” is
his least offence, perhaps, inasmuch as his legs
take very little part in it. The charm of the real
serpentine dance is in occasional disclosures of
female outline through the filmy covering. Yar-
mouth is not a base imitation of Mary Weir. He
is Said to wear Turkish pantaloons and other
clothes, but one can’t see much of the apparition
save the face, which is nosey and uninviting.
He confines himself to creating billowy effects
with two sticks. This he does very satis-
factorily, though a seven-foot automaton, by
the way, could do it much better. Length
of arm is everything in this mechanical
agitation of 48 yards of silk. As regards the
effective pictures thrown upon the drapery by a
magic lantern, if Lord Yarmouth were suspended
from a clothes-line in the centre of the stage ha
would answer the purpose equally well. Mel-
bourne sassiety will continue to hug the delusion
that a lantern slide reflected on an earl’s apparel
is a terpsiohorean triumph, in spite of the fore-
going remarks. But nobody will have the hardi-
hood to apologise for his attempts at acting. The
Bulletin judged him as an amateur in emotional
and farcical characters, and the sentence is unfit
for publication.

Melbourne Princess’ re-opened on Satur-
day, a scheme of fresh colour and a dream of new
delight. The whole interior of the house has been
decorated and upholstered up to date. It is good
to be there. “ Cinderella ” in this gorgeous place
is pretty much the same show that Sydney saw
recently, and coming after “Beauty and the
Beast ’’ will be reckoned a change for the better.
Horace Wheatley’s clog dancing, for which he
won a champion belt, or medal, or first-
prize certificate, a dozen years ago, favourably
affected a vast attendance; also his storio-i of
MTntyre were favorably received. Martyn
Hagan, as an ugly sister in rational dress, was
welcome, and James Nome, who simply came
forward and produced a sweet tenor voice from
behind an orthodox shirt-front, executed his mis-
sion with success. Marie Luella is the prince on
the present occasion, Ida Osborne in the name
part being another change from the Sydney cast.
Miss Lucy Fraser, and the perennial identity on
roller sljates. likewise a ventriloquist and" the
scenic effects, all passed off without a hitch.
Finally, the audience rose en masse , and paid a
debt of gratitude to Messrs. W. and M, for
omitting the harlequinade.

good concert by the Palmer-Beaumont co. fetched an
audience of, perhaps, 600 people. The result of the
collection was 4 pennies, 45 threepenny bits, 47 sixpences,
60 shillings, 8 florins and 5 half-crowns, about 400 visitors
contributing the sum of nix apiece. At a Melbourne hall,
where everybody is expected to put something in the
plate, a variety co.’s concert is about five times more
profitable.

The nigger-minstrel biz. has become a mania with many
Melb. suburban children. Several Collingwood young-
sters nigger-minstrelise in a large shed situated in a small
by-street, and their charge for admission, all over the
“ house,” is an empty beer-bottle ; after the performance
the bottles are sold to a bottle-merchant to pay show-
exes. A number of Geelong children, who certainly
deserve the cake, gave a minstrel show on Friday night
w'eek in a dilapidated shed at the rear of the Terminus
Hotel to a crowded “ house.” Their only means of adver-
tising is with chalk on footpaths “ Friday night, at the
back of the Terminus Hotel. The greatest show on earth.
All other minstrels outdone. Doors open at 7.30. Com-
mence at 8. Admission; Front seats, Id. ; back seats Jd.”

Most of the London musical agents are Hebrews.
Daniel Mayer, Melba’s agent, is one of them. Melba
herself looks like a Jewess, but she isn’t. Mayer is the
man who made a contract with R. S. Smythe for
Paderewski’s Australian tour. It was never carried out
but some day, probably, something will be“ heard about
it. The Much-Travelled is waiting till the clouds roll by.
People would not pay a guinea nowadays even to hear
the Pole with the fiery hair, and Paderewski’s red beard
will have grown grey before they will do so.
Mrs. Bryer, on the night of her ben. at the Opera
House, gave a bangle made of Chinese lucky-stone to
Clifford Ferley, who had worked energetically to make
the affair a success. At latest dates, Ferley was sleeping
in this hard, heathen decoration. He slipped the bangle
over his hand easily enough, but in consequence of its
luck-bringing properties, or something, there was no
taking it off again.
Lord Yarmouth, who has a Hebraic turn of the tomme
that harmonises with his cast of countenance, is likewise
keen in theatrical business matters. He showed com-
mendable anxiety to keep the exis. of Melb. benefit per-
formances as low as possible, although, in his private
capacity of earl, he provided champagne for the company
at rehearsal. 1 J
The awfulness of the “Morocco Bound” failure was
fully revealed in Melb. Practice Court, last week. Miss
Edna Grace sued those responsible for £5O arrears
of salary, which amount, according to the lady’s state-
ment, professed themselves unable to pay. She had
applied for her passage back to England, and they
couldn’t give her that, she said. She was penniless in a
strange land, etc. It has taken Edna Grace about three
months to bring her case up to the judgment stage
owing to “appearances” by the defendants and the
Xmas law vacation. No real defence was put in, and the
Court gave judgment with costs.

25 years old, and has been a circus star for 18 years
Three months ago some enemy drove a Sin. nail into the

that he didn't appear before Saturday last.

in London, if we can call the Crystal Palace London
After all, there is virtue in a good pair of understandings!

land.
A heavy actor in a Maoriland theatre got so very drunk
some months ago, that he was barely able to stagger to the
house about 6.30—n0t to apologise or to be pumped on
with a view of sobering up—but to guard his make-up
from his under-study and stop the show. With this view
he loaded two revolvers and lay in front of his room-door
defying all men to take away the props. His voice, which
resembles that of an angry bull in flv-time, soon brought
everybody on deck ; one woman fainted, others screamed-
the boss ordered him to surrender, but he said he would
blow, etc. He won. Finally a sub. was provided with a
faked-up dress.
Little Ethel, the wonderful baby of Woodlooh’s circus
has limbs and body of animakd elastic. She hangs bv
her heels from a trapeze 20ft. high ; holds the trapeze-bar
by her hands and does a double dislocation act which
would tear the limbs of an ordinary child out of their
sockets. Though she has been half of her little life a
public performer, she is as pretty and babyish as if she
had come from the nursery yesterday.

between Mrs. Patrick Campbell and manager George
Alexander, when “The Masqueraders” were on at St
James’s, London, Mrs. Pat. used to giggle at Alexander iii
the most critical moments. This was intolerable, and he
wrote thus to her : “Mr. Alexander presents his’compli-
ments to Mrs. Campbell and begs that she will not laugh
at him in the second act.” To which Mrs. Pat, replied:
“ Mrs. Patrick Campbell presents her compliments to Mr
Alexander and begs to say that she does not laugh at him
dm-i- g the second act. She always waits until she gets
home.” 6
Thus a N.Y. paper of Dec. 1 re the stage costume of a
charming actress who won many admirers in Australia-
“ln the third act of ‘Camille’ Olga Nethersole wears a
hall-drers, the bodice of which is kept in place by a pair
of pearl shoulder-straps. Sleeves there are none. Most
actresses never essay the part of CamiUfc until they are in
the neighborhood of 42. At that stage of the game it
would be impossible, not to say improvident, for them to
wear a duplicate of Miss Nethersole’s gown. Thanks to
her youth and beauty, Miss Nethersole fills the gown ad-
mirably, but, at the same time, the weight of responsi-
bility laid upon those strings is something dreadful to
contemplate.
With characteristic enterprise, Messrs. Williamson and
Musgrove have renovated and redecorated the Melbourne
Princess Theatre throughout at a cost exceeding A'3ooo,
and the re-beautified building was opened on Saturday
night with the burlesque extravaganza, “Cinderella.”
The renovations extend from cellar to ceiling 1 . The
decorations of the dome and the crush-room are very
beautiful art-works ; all the figures are hand-painted, and
have been done personally by Mr. Phil. Goatcher. In a
word, the restoration is so complete and thorough as to
render the Princess practically a new theatre. Carpels
alone have cost over £6OO, and the beautiful blue plush
in which the dress-circle is being upholstered must have
run into many shekels. Under the electrio-light, the
theatre glitters and sparkles like a palace of pleasure—a
dream-picture from a romantic fairyland.
“Billy Bind,” Sydney’s veteran theatrical manager,
whose decease at the age of 82 has just been regret fully
chronicled, was born in London on April 12. 1813. When
he finished his schooling he spent 16 months iu West
Africa, fighting niggers; afterwards coming to Tasmania,
and thence to Australia, where almost immediately he
became acting-manager of the old Victoria Theatre—of
which, and of the Prince of Wales’ Theatre, he was
subsequently lessee. He muained conned ed with
the latter theatre for a period of over 32 years, until it
was burnt down on Jan. 16, 1872. During this lengthy
term of management he introduced to Australia many
celebrated artists, including Barry Sullivan, the Dons, the
Marsh troupe, Bandmann, Lyster’s Opera Co., and others
well-known in their day. When Mr. Diud retired from
theatrical life, he took an hotel at North Shore, and
became an alderman and for five years mayor of that
municipality. He was a genial, hearty old gentleman, of
stainless honor; and the “ profession ” has never had a bet-
ter friend. Mr. Dind’s portrait appears in this wei k’s Bul-
letin. “Melh. Pro.” writes apropos: Billy Dind was the
most positively last of the Romans, unless I forget
Charley Burford. Billy carried Sydney with him, even to
Melh., and there never was a more Sydneyish man. What
a fine Turveydropism about him, combined with real
Orummlesian grandeur of presence ! But he was never
fraudy, and always conveyed the impression of having
well-dined. He was emphatically a fine man in h ; s day
There were two events in his life—the combustions of the
Prince of Wales’s. He never could make Providence out.
Billy must always have a grand vizier of the Sam Lazar
order; but then there was the appeal to the potentate,
who delighted in acts of mercy. He had the head, per-
haps, of Coppin, if you extract Ooppin’s mobility. Dind
never got beyond the date of somewhere about 1858.
Monarch of all he surveyed in the days when Australian
Thespians formed a close borough, like Buckstone’s Hay.
market Co. There was never an old man but Rogers
never a low comedian but Charley Young. The old man
was a mine of anecdotes about the recently-released con-
vict, Bertrand. The little parlor in the right wing of
Dind’s old hotel on the rise of the hill from Milsou's Point
was Kinder’s boozing-ground and afterwards the scene of
the inquest on his body, and thither the dentist culprit
used to betake himself to “ take care” of his victim.

The postal conference has done one
memorable thing—resolved unanimously “ that it
be made a condition of any future contracts that
mail-boats be manned with white labor only.”
Now, let the Postmasters show they have spunk
enough to stick to their resolution, for un-
doubtedly strong pressure will be brought to make
them change it. The cheap colored laborer is the
protege of every Fat Man in the universe.
A recent fire completely consumed the
model prison, the old Government House, gaol
hospital and public buildings of that quondam
hell-on-earth, Port Arthur. In these days of
psychological spookery, imagination depicts the
ghosts of the old tortured victims of "The
System ” each being let out of limbo for one brief
hour to apply a blazing brand to their former hell.
Would that some fire-miracle would orcould efface
at once and for ever the hellish legacies of “The
System ” still visible in Australasia !
The Bulletin has received a long letter—
far too long to print—from “Jus,” controverting
the allegation that the evidence on which the
convict Grenon was lately hanged was unsatisfac-
tory. So far as this paper is aware, no one has
impugned the hona-fides of either watchman
Heavey or the arresting constable. The point to
be urged against Heavey is that he may have been
wounded by his own pistol and, in the excitement
of the struggle, have mistaken the bullet as com-
ing from Grenon’s. Or that it was a case of mis-
taken identification, the real criminal being still
at large.
The sea between Sydney Heads, last
Saturday, was so tremendous as to stop the ferry-
steamers running. Neither of the rival companies
has a boat absolutely fit to face the huge beam
sea which comes of an easterly gale. What is
wanted for the work is a powerfully-engined
whale-backed tug, with plenty of passenger-
accommodation and no top-hamper. Over and
over again steamers have been temporarily dis-
abled between the Heads through derangement of
the steering-gear or break-down of the engines.
One rough morning there will be a break-down in
a big sea, and then Manly cemetery will be full of
what the sharks have left, and the insurance-
companies will pay out a couple of hundred-
thousand in one lump.
The Victorian revenue for January is
<£76,764 less than the badly-diminished revenue
for the same month of 1894, which means that it
is still falling at the cheerful rate of £900,000 per
annum. Carter, in ’93, produced his beautiful
Budget of Hope, showing that the public income
was going to rise again, and complained because
“fuels groaned enormous groans and prophesied
evil tilings. As it happened, the revenue fell
immensely, and when, in ’94, Carter brought in
his next Budget of Hope, showing another
expected rise, the House threw him out. Then
1 urner fixed up a baleful and pessimistic estimate
of revenue which, so far, is proving almost strictly
accurate. Turner can claim to be the first really
successful Treasurer the province has had since
the days of—well, The Bulletin can’t remember
the name of the last one.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback— the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted te
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who make*
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.
The Wild-Cat Column .
The Union Bank—that good, solid, old English
Institution which sends so much money “home” to be
distributed in dividends in the dear old mother country-
pays a div. of 6 per cent., and reports having a cash
reserve of £5,000,000.
* • • *
Deposits in Vic. and N.S. W. banks continue to
unfix themselves. Most customers continue to leave
their gold in the institutions at call. Perhaps it is
because they don’t want to saddle the banks with in-
terest, and perhaps it is because they think they can
whip the cash out of a current account at the first sign
Of a financial storm. In the last three months of ’94
N.S.W. fixed deposits decreased by £1,367,733-the cur-
rent accounts meanwhile increasing by £1,111,829. On
the whole, N.S. W. hanks lost a quarter of a million in
deposits in the quarter. Vic. banks had a gain, on the
whole, of £76,000; while the fixed deposits decreased by
£511,055, the current accounts increased by £587,308.
These figures form an almost pathetic picture of the
popular anxiety concerning the fate of the banks in the
coming time of storm. Meanwhile the banks adhere to
the old polioy of drift. Sufficient unto the stolen steed
of yesterday is the locked door of to-morrow.
* * * *
The Bank of Van Diemen’s Land has got on so
far with its liquidation that the liabilities have been
reduced from £960,000 to £352-,000. The assets still re-
maining are nominally valued at £452,000, but in cases of
this kind the toughest and most unpromising assets
usually remain to the last, and very little hope is enter-
tained that they will cover the remaining debts. Every-
thing depends on the luck which attends the big lottery,
but the lottery has progressed rather slowly, and has
been hanging fire for a long and dreary while.
* » » *
So far, £ 151,000 worth of Maoriland per
cent, consols have been sold locally at par. These are
better terms than any Australasian Government has ever
made in the London market, with the exception of one
transaction which Victoria effected in the boom time ;and
the fact furnishes the several hundredth reason for avoid-
ing the London market hereafter.
* • ♦ *
The A.J.S. Bank, it is now announced, proposes
to meet each of the ten instalments of the reconstructed
deposits in the same way as the first, by paying one-half
and renewing the other for five years, or so. The follow-
ing obscure and slipshod announcement also appears in
the report: “The directors believe that the amount
placed aside to provide for the losses upon the business of
the hank will be sufficiently covered by the reserve-funds;
this conclusion is formed after again quite recently con-
sidering the position of our affairs.” This apparently
means that the old reserve-fund of £700,000, or so, will be
sufficient to cover the bank’s bad debts and depreciated
securities. The half-yearly meeting passed off without
any explanation of the fearful and unaccountable falling
off in the profits, so that enlightenment on this point
seems to be indefinitely postponed—which is a pity.
There is nothing about the institution which wants ex-
plaining half so badly.

N.S.W. Postal officials state that during last
year the issue of money-orders has much increased.
The reason appears to be that many people being refused
any more bank credit have recourse to the somewhat
clumsy method of sending and receiving money through
the P. 0., as if they deposited a copper with the bank it
might be snapped up and credited against O.D.

formed some wonderful gymnastic feats to reach the
manager’s room. And all he desired to be assured of was
that his overdraft was all right 1 He wanted to know if
the bank would run away with what he owed it!

Dear Bulletin, —The very wild-cat writer on
Trade Protection Societies displays both ignorance and
malice. If his assertion be correct (that the societies
grade the financial status of their subscribers according
to the rate of the subscribers’ subscription fees), how
long do you think would those societies last? Three
(ninths is, as a rule, quite long enough for any business
firm to ascertain if the information supplied by the
society be true or false, and it would not take six months
to ruin any Trade Protection Society which depends on
the accuracy of its reports were that society to act as
suggested. The very fact that two societies have existed
honorably in Sydney for between 20 to 30 years, three in
Melbourne from 6 to 25 years, and that these five concerns
have had, ail that time, the full confidence of bankers,
merchants, and the legal profession, gives your cor-
respondent the lie. And I may add that, to my certain
knowledge (so far as Melbourne is concerned), not one of
ithe three reputable societies there will take a “ bad egg ”
ias a subscriber at any price.—Vosnibara.
