According to Minister “Works” Young, on
Feb. 1, there was not one man in N.S.W. employed on the
construction of any State railway ; indeed, with the
exception of a small private line at Parramatta, there was
not a man employed on railway construction at all.
Which accounts, in some degree, for the summer over-
supply of unemployed.

Vigorous efforts are to be made by the share-
holders of several Australian institutions to bring about
The election of auditors by the members instead of by the
■directors. How the system, now so common, originated
■it is difficult to say, but that it lends itself admirably to a
dishonest or incompetent directorate is universally felt.

♦ » » *
Two banks are already grieved, and other
creditors will be wild, to learn that one of the reputedly
soundest of Southern wine and spirit houses is hope-
lessly insolvent. The prominent position which has
been held by one of the firm appears to have facilitated
both the discounting of “supported” paper and the
obtaining of large trade credits. Boiled down: The
banks may get 10s. in the £, and the trade creditors have
a very slender hope of getting 2s. 6d.
* ♦ • •
Since the first of the year an important bank
has deposed four country managers for the following
reasons: —(1) Advancing too much on pastoral and pro-
duce securities. (2)—(26 miles away from No. ])—Not
employing the bank’s capital profitably, and declining
undoubtedly good pastoral and produce securities. (3)
Giving storekeepers too big overdrafts on their farmers’
hills. (4)—(20 miles from No. 3)—Not encouraging store-
keepers and farmers to utilise the institution’s money,
and thus eating his head off (by way of salary) at the
hank’s expense.

• * * *
The Balmain New Ferry Co.’s balance-sheet for
the year is all rose-color. Receipts, £8393, an increase of
£213 on previous year ; expenses, £6757, a decrease of
£333—despite circumstances which might legitimately
have put the figures the other way about. Adding £294
from last year, the gross profits available were £1929.
£998 was deducted for depreciation in value of boats, and
a div. at the rate of 6 per cent, per ann. paid for last half-
year, absorbing £342. This leaves £563 to carry forward.
Some shareholders thought it would have been better
policy to postpone the div. and add another boat to the
company’s Heet ; but a large number of small investors
wanted to »ee some cash return for their mon»y, and as
the co. is now likely to have a monopoly of the traffic, its
position is assured as long as it is managed as capably as
at present.

Thus Tozer, the Brazen, to a Tasmanian scribe ;
“The interest due on 30th June is waiting in London—
in gold. We have also some two millions in the bank
bearing interest.” True, Horace, true ! There is some
two millions in the bank, and well we know it. We also

* • * »
A rise in Victorian per cents, moved Melb.
Age to print the following as cable news from its own
correspondent: “The hears who have been depressing
the stock are having rebukes heaped upon them by the
public, who, alarmed at the turn taken by the market,
were induced to sell at low prices.” Heavens and earth !
The old gents, and ladies who buy Australian stock as an
investment don’t know bears from bulls, and they most
certainly are not in the habit of heaping rebukes upon
either class of animal when the price of a “ bread and
cheese ” article rises or falls 1 per cent. The public in
this connection are buyers and holders, not gamblers ; and
stock-exchange manoeuvres have an interest only for the
trade.

Last month was, as regards ready cash, the
tightest experienced by most Melbourne houses for
months past.
* * * •
Many Melbourne commercial brokers are now
not making “tucker.” One in particular, whose turn-
over some years ago averaged £300,000 per annum, is not
earning £1 a week. In the grain-trade, where £1
commission was allowed on every hundred bags sold,
the “com.” is now half-a-crown.
♦ * * «
A great many unpatriotic Australians make a
point of doing business only with English or other
foreign fire-insurance companies, out of a childlike belief
in their superior stability. Some years ago, several Aus-
tralian firms, which had fire claims on a certain British
institution, made a serious loss when that lamented con-
cern declared its inability to pay, and closed its doors ;
and some others will meet a similar by-and-
bye. At least five foreign companies doing business in
Australia are exceedingly feeble and rickety affairs, which
would go under with slight provocation ; and 19 of them
are, by comparison with the two leading Australasian
offices, very second-rate concerns indeed. A little less of
that child-like faith in the solvency of everything that
isn’t Australian would do this country a lot of good.
♦ * * *
A well-known “sport,” who, for some time
past, has been keeping a Melb. pub., got into arrears for
rent to the tune of £75. His landlord tried to eject him,
hut was always met with “The license is in my name; how
will you get another ? ” The only compromise the tenant
would agree to was a clear receipt for all overdue rent
and £5O cash for the surrender of the license. And he
carried the day.
* ♦ ♦ ♦
Melb. landlords have some hot tenants. The
week before last a man who had been defying his land-
lord, the law and its minions, for weeks, had the roof
lifted from over his head. But even then he wouldn’t
quit until the landlord had promised in writing to allow
him his furniture.
* • ♦ *
Manly Co-operative Ferry Co.’s refusal to
amalgamate with the old co. (forced by competition to
reduce its return fare from Is. 6d. to 6d., to the
great gain of the public) rouses anonymous corres-
pondents in the dailies to viciously suggest that
“racing” should be prohibited, and that “safe and
proper accommodation should be provided for pas-
sengers.” The “racing” refers to the fact that one of
the new co.’s boats is faster than those of the old co.,
causing the latter some loss of revenue. The Marine
Board is satisfied as to the safety of the new co.’s boats,
and if the public were not satisfied as to the quality of
accommodation it wouldn’t patronise them. Fact is, the
sixpenny traffic is beginning to lessen. The new co. is
not dead, as prophesied, hut, on the contrary, is increas-
ing its capital so as to purchase better boats ; and the
ghost of the old Balmain Ferry Co. has been seen round
Manly lately.
• * * *
The outlook at Wyalong is much brighter than
it has been for some time. At least half-a-dozen good
claims have been bought privately within the last few
weeks by a Vic. syndicate, which, it is stated, intends erect-
ing the latest and best plant.
* * * •
B.H. Proprietary yield for the last half-year
(Nov. 30) was 6,767,0560 z, or less by 520,2810 z. than that
of the preceding period, which was the highest on record.
The average contents of the ore have declined steadily
since the co. started. For the first half-year (Nov.
30, ’B5) the total output was 48 tons of ore, averaging
741’770z. per ton. Last half-year the figures were 320,095
tons, averaging 20 - 760 z., the lowest average on record.
The average prices obtained were also the lowest on
record. So, moreover, were the wages paid to the work-
men.
* » • •
The average gold-mine becomes poorer with
age, and especially with increased tonnage. Baker’s
Creek is down to about an oz. to the ton. Bamfleld, one
of the directors, has wired that there are four years’ ore
in sight—of Tittle use if it is not worth an oz. to the ton.
♦ * ♦ ♦
Howell estimates there are a million tons of
oxidised ore left in the Broken Hill mine, which will
maintain the present div. As to the other bodies, he
made it clear enough that divs. can only come by a great
reduction in the cost of treatment and in mining ex-
penses, or by a rise in silver, or a big rise in lead—both
remote contingencies.
Concerning the recent Bulletin inquiry for a
half-ounce mine there have been many more respondents
than mines. A number have been examined and found
wanting in the half-oz.—or a reef big enough to make
half an oz. pay. In three cases the owners or agents,
the latter always sincere amateurs, would have one
believe that a three, four or five-ton selected crushing
was a guarantee of the average value of the reef. Only
one correspondent knocked the writer off his perch. His
reply was : “ My father and myself have been looking for
30 years for what you want; a half-oz. mine in a good-sized
reef. We think we have at last found one and, take my
word, we intend to stick to it. We are now opening it
up.” When a mine is for sale it is generally a case of
“ closing up.”

Somebody has picked up a 104 oz. specimen a
little way from Bayley’s. Recent experiences caused
Ba.yley’s people to view this find with some slight jealousy.
They don’t pick up 104 oz. specimens—now.
* * * *
Many sensational finds may be expected within
150 miles of Coolgardie, while rains enable prospectors to
get away from the main water-tracks. Nine out of ten
prospectors have hitherto been compelled to confine their
work within an easy distance of water-holes. Another
Londonderry and great will be the London boom.
* * * *
Hogan’s Gully, Coolgardie, where the 104 oz. slug
has been found, is 300 yards north of Bayley’s and on
higher ground. It has been turned over by dry blowers,
who found hundreds of small slugs, but missed the
boomer, which was just unearthed by the recent rains.
The reefs around Hogan’s Gully—and there are several of
them—consist of snow-white barren quartz.
* * * *
Another “ new find ” reported from Coolgardie
—which has more promising infants and sick adults than
any other goldfield past or present. This particular dis-
covery is 30 miles N.W. of the township and 12 miles
from the Wealth of Expectations. There are the usual
rich specimens, and big, solid reef *• studded with coarse
gold.” Various circumstances, however, have contributed
to exhaust Eastern capacity for enthusiasm until bulk
crushings appear.
* • * •
On 13th Jan., a roll-up was called at Siberia,
Westralia, to investigate the alleged theft of BJoz. gold
and a silver watch, by a man in custody who, it was
claimed, had been almost caught in the act. On pressure
he at last acknowledged having taken the articles. In
his tent were found the two missing nuggets, one of 7
and the other of lioz., which were at once restored to the
owner. The arrestee, who then acknowledged having
hid/ien the watch in a hollow log, was divested of his
sh&ith-knife and pistol, and a rope having been tied
round his neck he was led forth in quest of the watch,
which, after a search of an hour-and-a-half, he failed to
find—whereupon Judge Lynch ordered him to pay its
value in money. This having been done he was marched
back to the main camp, tried, and ordered to hand ijver
l)oz. gold to Coolgardie hospital, and quit the field in
two hours, which he did. He is averred to be an old
offender. When he left Siberia he had about 250 z. in
nuggets. This is the first roll-up in Siberia, and, despite
the gentleness of the proceedings, it is hoped it will be a
warning to thieves.

The miners at Dundas are trying to get the
Westralian Govt, to duplicate the Coolgardie telegraph
service (which now runs via Perth and Albany to Esper-
anoe Bay), by building a line from Coolgardie through
Dundas to the same port. The Forrest following, how-
ever, having no interest in Dundas, are blocking the
scheme.
* * » »
It is acknowledged by a leading Westralian
manager that if in the early days the real tonnage put
through a 10-head battery per fortnight had been made
public, the |oz. return would have so frightened share-
holders that no further calls would have been paid. Thus,
120 tons a fortnight with 10-head returned 130 oz. Now,
with 20-head, 500 tons a fortnight, same quality stone,
return about 200 oz. Are many of the Coolgardie managers
on this tack ? How would the market be if the report
read not “ 130 tons, 5000 z.,” but “ 260 tons, 300 oz. ?”

Star of the East, Cue, 400 tons for 2500 z., and
it is explained by a newspaper correspondent that the
quality of the quartz decreases as they work away from
the shoot of gold. It generally does ; but they don’t
usually work away from shoots -at least no manager like
Capt. Dick, who is boss of the mine, wpuld think of doing

cut down. Mine now on London market.
» ♦ * *
The W.A. Govt, begins to blow about the
money already expended on the new goldfields in water-
conservation. Nine-tenths of the amount was spent in
dams, apd most of it wasted. The recent heavy rains did
not half fill thetp, and more water is lost by absorption
than by use. But it is beginning to dawn on them that
the granite-country, which holds the water at the soaks,
runs from one soak to another beneath the sand, where
enormous quantities of fresh and brackish water are
retained because of the almost entire absence of surface-
channels to carry it away.
♦ » ♦ *
As long anticipated, typhoid and low-fevers are
more prevalent in Coolgardie after the rains, especially'
among men whose systems have run down. The conden-
sers are idle, and people are drinking water caught over
necessarily the filthiest catchment-area in Australia.

A Chinese claim at Port Darwin (where nine
claims in ten are Chinese) has had a Xmas crushing of
11 tons, 2640 z. Last Xmas it was 34 tons, 6300 z. This is
the sort of thing that fills tbe Japs with cheerful expecta-
tions of a big war-subsidy—for every ounce goes straight
to China.

The tributors of the Kapunga mine, Coro
mandel, M.L., 24 hours before the expiration of their
tribute, came on a rich patch and got £6OOO from 6o'wt.
specimens. The mine-owners stood by with watches
waiting for midnight, when work closed. From 20 loads
of general stuff the tributors got an additional 2500 z. ;
total, 21460 z, The co. has taken possession, and the
chances are it will not get as much gold in 24 weeks. It
requires time to find a patch ; very little to work it out.
» * » »
At the little Vic. town of Briagoloug, gold has
just been found, in unheard-of circumstances, on the
Gladstone river. The gold is in appearance like nothing
ever seen in Vic. before. It has a whitey'-yellow look,
and is, in its native state, of low specific gravity. A casual
glance discovers the unusual color ; a moment’s handling
brings the second surprise at its low weight; but careful
inspection brings the greatest surprise of ail.-
The gold is in layers like the leaves of a book.
Of course, the layers cannot be readily separated except
here and there, where the edges are curly—dog's-eared.
Subsequent to its stratification the metal has, of course,
been under great pressure, and a piece here and there is
not much different to flattened-out gold from ordinary
quartz as occasionally found. But a handful of the pieces
as taken from the sandstone look like nothing in the
golden way. Furthermore, they rustle and rattle, do
these strange pieces, like autumn leaves or tinsel.
Reginald Murray and Secretary Howitt both
experience strange sensations in their geological hearts
when the subject is mentioned to them. To them the
discovery of gold in upper Devonian rocks is a new vista
in geology as well as in mining. This is written after an
inspection of the gold and in a moment of, perhaps,
enthusiasm, but it does seem as if there should be plenty
more upper Devonian rocks available for gold deposits.
Murray and Howitt are requested to immediately produce
more auriferous upper Devonian rocks. Australia wants
them badly.
• • * *
Certain small, co-operative parties who are
working on the lease of a pretty old Northern co., are
compelled to crush at the battery of the co., which draws
good divs. in consequence. Some time ago the stone
from two of the parties got mixed, half of each being
tipped into the Wrong box. The stone of one usually
cleaned up poor—that from the other was richer. Nothing
was said about the mistake until the usual clean-up. It
was then found that the returns did not differ a single
grain from those of previous crushings! Those miners
are now strongly of opinion that the system of public
crushing wants looking into.

The Thames (M.L.) deep-levels, which H. A.
Severn, 20 years ago, prophesied would be found to he
highly auriferous, are to be prospected with English
capital. Rich gold was carried down 400 or 500 feet, and
then came a barren stratum.

Thompson’s Stock and Shark Report for
February contains a strong reproof directed against those
“brigands of finance and company-wreckers” who failed
to enthuse, like other right-thinking persons, over the
magnificent prospects of the A.J.S. Bank. The report of
that institution, it says, shows “ signs of growing
strength,” and it is also solving the reconstruction pro-
blem on the lines it laid down. A certain amount of
assurance is required to talk itrthis strain about a concern
whose profits have fallen nearly 60 peir cent. In one year,
whose shapes have dropped 60 per cent, in the same time,
whose dividend has just been reduced, and whose direc-
tors, instead of solving the reconstruction problem on
the lines laid down, are already negotiating for an exten-
sion of time. Still, the necessity for “restoring confi-
dence,” no matter whether the confidence is well or
ill-founded, doubtless excuses everything.

The late W. J. McGaw, of Kooba station,
N.S.W., has left £82,550 —all to his wife. Dur-
ing his latter days he lived chiefly at Manly, in
“Dailey’s castle,” which he bought some years
ago, and figured as a beneficent supporter of the
local Presbyterian church.

“HILLY” DIN D, the veteran ex-theatrical-
manager.
After the Sydney Public Library, the
largest and best collection of works on Australasia
is possessed by Mr. David Mitchell, of Darling-
hurst-road, Sydney. Mr. Mitchell, a barrister by
profession, is substantially wealthy, and makes
book-collection his life’s hobby.
Andy Kelly, ex-M.L.A., now catering at
Wyalong, could, had he liked, have been lifted
into a brewer’s pub., in his old constituency, at
the close of his N.S.W. legislative career. It was
understood that he dreaded having to supply free
drinks to a couple of thousand voters.
Prof Morris’s “Life of Higinbotham,”
which was to have appeared some time ago, will
not be out for a few months yet. The book will
make about GOO pp., and will deal pretty fully
with the stirring political struggles in which the
late Chief Justice played sa prominent a part.

Some people are predicting that S.A.
Premier Kingston will take a title when it is
offered him. The Bulletin presumes that King-
ston could have had a title long ago had he chosen,
as he is the sort of man whom it would pay the
Imperial party to bribe. He is the son of a
knight, but if he himself should accept a bauble it
would be time for The Bulletin to cut its throat.
The senior practitioner under the S.A.
Medical Board is Dr. J. H. Browne (brother of
wealthy squatter Dr. W. J. Browne, who lately
died in London). He was registered 4th Jan.,
1845, his name being tenth on the list—the late
Dr. Mayo (28th Dec., 1844) was the seventh. Four
physicians registered before 1850 still survive—
namely, Dr. Phillips, of Adelaide; Dr. Taylor
(father of Lieut.-Governor Way’s aide-de-camp),
Dr. M. P. Hayward, and Dr. Browne—all of
whom live in England.
Photo. by Vandyke] [ Melbourne ,
Miss EFFIE STILLWELL, a Melbourne
Mathematician.
Miss Effie Stillwell, the brilliant young
Melbourne scholar whose portrait appears here-
with, is a gentle, unaffected little damsel with soft
blue eyes and shy dimples coming and going in
her pale cheeks. Despite her childlike appear-
ance, the sweet girl genius is “ nearly eighteen,”
as she is careful to mention with triumphant
emphasis on the “nearly.” Alas ! pride in one’s
advancing age is doomed to have a fall. At the
age of seven, Miss Effie began her scholastic
career, previously delayed by wise parental
anxiety. A year later she decided on becoming a
doctor, and in pursuance of this long-treasured
ambition, she will, in a few weeks, enter on the
Melb. University’s medical course. The interval
between seven and seventeen has been for the
enthusiastic student a period of hard work, illu-
mined by a series of successes, notably a State-
school scholarship, matriculation, South Mel-
bourne College dux-ship, Ormond Exhibition, and
just the other day the matriculation mathe-
matical exhibition and first-class science honors.
Though this sounds formidable, nobody can be
more unlike the popular conception of the “New
Woman ” than this unassuming Victorian girl,
who, so far, evidently has not realised that the
men deserve stamping-out. On the contrary,
replying to a question re her beloved South Melb.
College, where both sexes are taught together,
she gave it as her opinion that the boys would
outshine the girls if they worked as hard, which,
in that stronghold of football, they generally do
not. Miss Stillwell, as before stated, won this
year’s mathematical exhibition, but she regards
this class of subjects purely as a grateful relief and
relaxation from medical studies. Her mathe-
matical ability is said to be inherited from Mrs.
Stillwell. The maternal talents are said to have
descended in even more marked degree upon
a second daughter, for whom still greater achieve-
ments are prophesied.
One good point about N.S.W. Chief-Com-
missioner for Railways—he won’t have his men
bluffed by the legislative snob. “ Look kere,” he
said, when one pumpkin-headed terror of tram-
guards had made his hundredth charge or so,
“ I’ve had enough of this ! My men ean’t be
always in the wrong, and, in future, when the
guard tells you he doesn’t know you, you’ll just
have to take his word and show your pass, or be
put off the tram like an ordinary human being ! ”
An attempt is being made to get a British
civil-list pension for the widow of the late Pro-
fessor Pearson (erstwhile Victorian Minister for
Public Instruction), who has been left quite on-
provided for. Poor as Victoria is, and vicious as
is the general pension principle, the local Demo-
cratic-party might do worse than support a State-
grant to Mrs. Pearson, whose able and scholarly
husband did inestimable service for the country
both as a journalist and as a politician, in days
when democrats from the ranks of “the classes”
were so few as to be pearls above price.
Henry Louis Bertrand, the Kinder assassin
—recently liberated from more than a “natural
life’s ” doom in gaol on condition that he exiled
himself from Australia until the death of a certain
exalted personage—has determined, now that the
potentate is dead, to return to Sydney. This, on
the authority of private letters. Bertrand has
now, it is said, inherited considerable means. The
programme is in perfect accordance with one
unfolded to the writer during a gaol interview
with Bertrand some three years ago, in which
interview the reality of the existence of “ that
terrible diary ” was insisted upon.

mouth may be ex-

Story told by the enemies of Minister of
Labor W. P. Reeves (M.L.), who lately visited
Australia. He returned pers.s. Port Melbourne,
which is manned by a Lascar crew. The heart of
W.P.R. sickened at the sight of these dark faces,
and he was not seen on deck for several days.
When at last he ventured up, he held forth to the
captain on the subject, predicting that the day
would soon come when all ships would have to be
manned by men of his own race and colour. The
captain listened patiently and deferentially, but
in silence. Coming on deck next morning, he
found the Minister of Labor reclining luxuriously
on a deck chair, while a Lascar lad was humbly
tying the ministerial shoe-strings. “Ah,” said
Captain Richardson, “you would not get seamen
of your own race and color to do that! ”
Douglas Sladen tells this story. A certain
member of Melb. Club one day, some years ago,
arrived there so drunk that the hall-porter thought
it inexpedient that he should enter. After long
persuasion he induced him to go away in a cab
ostensibly bound for home, but the old gentleman
was on pleasure bent and ultimately got himself
locked up by a policeman. Whilst they were
taking the usual charge of drunk and disorderly,
the Superintendent happened to come in and at
once saw what was the matter. After he had held
a whispered conversation with the bobby the
latter went out and shortly returned with a hope-
lessly-incapable drunk who was promptly lodged
in the cells, the Club man being sent home in a
cab. Next morning the unfortunate drunk found
himself charged with being drunk and disorderly
and personating Mr. Thomas , of W
Park.
Another Australian writer has been dis-
covered by T. P. O’Connor, of London Sun :
During- the last few months, Mr. Frank Donohue, one
of the rising race of young Australian writers, has been
visiting us in London. Mr. Donohue, up to the present,
has been mainly employed in journalism in his native
country, and is regarded as one of the keenest and most
discriminating literary Critics of the Antipodes. His
visit to London was associated with a more ambitious
enterprise than mere work-a-day journalism. In the
library of the Brit sh Muieum he was able to realise a
long-cherished dream. He has “discovered” the real
pioneer and hero of Antipodean exploration. De Quiros,
in Mr. Donohue’s view, deserves to receive the same
place in the Valhalla of Australia as Columbus in that of
America. Indeed, Mr. Donohue has been much struck by
the many points of analogy between the views, adven-
tures, troubles, and final triumph of the two explorers. I
look forward with interest to the volume, in which the old
world history and figures will reappear.
Mr. Donohue, who was formerly a Sydney G.P.O.
official, began his literary career on The Bulle-
tin, and is identical with “Arthur Gayll,” author
of “ The History of Botany Bay.” He has of late
years been an esteemed member of the S.M.
Herald staff.
A DARWINIAN THEORY.
Possible appearance of the Australian youth
after a few cricket-seasons.
The death of ex-Chief Justice Cockle, of
Queensland (heserved from 1862 to 1879) saves the
province one of two judicial pensions of .£1250 a
year. The other is paid to Charles Lilley, who,
however, spends the money where it is raised ;
while Mr. Cockle left for England immediately
on the completion of his term of service, and his
very name is unknown to most Queenslanders.
The deceased ex-Chief Justice (who was “closely
related to Cockle’s pills ”) was a pleasant ol^
gentleman whose latter-day hobby was mathe-
matics. A few years ago he was President of the,
Savage Club, London.
It is related that a Maoriland potentate
once travelled some hundreds of miles per coasting
steamer with two friends “to hide Monty
Browne.’’ That steamer called in at seven or eight
intermediate ports, and it so happened that each
of five of Monty’s brothers was sub-editor at
one of these ports, while Monty was sub. at the
last. Towns were small, and steamers “events”'
in those times. Bobadil with his two mates called!
ashore at each port and let “ the sub.” know his
errand. The fun grew expectant as Monty got

YARMOUTHS MELBOURNE DEBUT.
What’s in a name ? A vain, young, stage-struck
chappie,
His voice unmusical, quite passionless and;
weak :
Void of dramatic instinct, lacking humor happy,
And everything an actor needs, save “cheek,’'
Gives a performance free from all suggestion
Of e’en the art most amateurs may claim,
People applaud, the while they dodge the ques-
tion—
What’s in a name ?
What’s in a name ? Rank-glamour, grovel-mad-
ness,
A vile disease that drags men’s reason low :
Else had the crowd turned sorrow into gladness
By making merry at Lord Yarmouth’s show.
No peals of laughter mocked his feeble mumming,
No god-hurled turnip oast light-hearted blame —
Though better men have fled from bottles coming.
What’s in a name !

POLITICAL
POINTS.
A politician is an in stitution for the pro-
stitution of the con-stitution.
Motto of N.S.W. Labor Electoral League :
“ United we planned ; divided we brawl.”
“The Conference of Premiers is mere
vapor—and very foul vapor at that.”—H. Parkes.
Three schemes of Federation are now on
the political racing track—the Federal Council’s,
the Premiers’ (which is Dr. Quick’s), and the Syd-
ney Convention’s.
The Bulletin does not want to see an
Australian Judge on the English Bench. Bub it
would cheerfully vote thousands to see some of
the English Judges on the Australian Bench.
The absence “out of bounds” of the
N.S.W. Attorney-General and his Deputy loudly
proclaims the fact that the Attorney-General
should be a civil-servant and not a political mate.
The Australian Ministry least representa-
tive of the people of i ts province is John Forrest’s,
and no surprise or disappointment should be felt
that it is least enthusiastic in the forward Federa-
tion movement.
Premier Kingston’s private views on
Federation are emphatic. “ What is the use of
Victoria asking S. A. to federate when she totally
ignores all appeals to settle the vexed disputed
boundary ? ” he said recently.
So far S.A. village settlements have been
a great success, and although one or two are very
shaky, notone has been broken up. S.A. Govt,
hopes to absorb the shaky ones in its Lake Bonney
irrigation colony, now in the initiatory stages.
The first and successful public auction of
South Australian wine from the State-supervised
depot in London marks a momentous era in the
history of Australia. It is the destiny of this
country to supply wine for hundreds of millions
outside our boundaries. The Prohibitionists are
g.nawing a file.
Premier Kingston wants Way to act as
Lieut.-Governor for at least 12 months ; but the
Imperial Govt, is obdurate and intends to appoint
Kintore’s successor next April. Awhile ago
Kingston said he would, under these circum-
stances, ask Parliament not to vote more than
Way’s salary of £2500 to the new Governor, and
now people are wondering whether he will do
this. Kintore’s successor will, in any case, have
to nav all salaries frc out of his screw of £5OOO.

The Vic. Railway Department’s
circular requesting ex-M.’sL.A. to re-
turn their free passes without delay,
has brought in answers whose general
tenor is that the ex-members will see
the department further before they
part with them. Some ask to be
allowed to keep the passes as memen-
toes —and, presumably, to travel on
them. Some refuse to send them in,
on the ground that they have had
them made into trinkets for their
she-relatives. One politician, it has
been ascertained, sold his pass to a
publican, or else left it as security for
an orgie, and then got another on the
ground that the original was lost, and
now refuses to even return the dupli-
cate, though he has been long out of
the Assembly. The climax is reached
in the case of an infuriated person
who threatens to smash the Commis-
sioner “wid his fisht” if anymore
questions are asked. This gentleman
is understood to be in dead earnest,
and interesting developments are
anticipated.
The vexed sects which denounce the Sun-
day sitting of N.S.W. L.E.L. conference as pro-
fanity do not take into account the fact that in
France, Belgium, and several other countries, the
Parliamentary elections are always held on
Sundays.
Young lawyer Byrnes (Q.), at the Federal
Council, Hobart (Tas.), referred deliciously to the
“Premiers’ Conference ” as “an informal meeting
of persons in the same city.” Not for nothing
did Byrnes read for a year with Pat Real, now
J;udge Real.
1 Archbishop Carr, of Melbourne ; “It is
greatly to be feared that after a few years the
population of Victoria outside the R.O. Church
will be pagan.” The Archbishop takes too rosy a
view. According to the ideas of reverends a
pagan is a man who has no parson.
The now notoriously-clamored-for Duff-
Dibl a correspondence is to be moved for in the
British House of Commons—at the instance, no
doubt, of ex-Premier Dibbs. Though the N.S.W.
Parliament has ordered the correspondence,
administrative guile is suspected of keeping it
back under various pleas.
Henry Parkes declares that if there were
“in power” “four honest, capable men in Aus-
tralia, Australia could be federated in a year.”
Oh, that “ Hif! ” The mind’s eye can see the
possible honest and capable four, but, alas ! they
are “bout!” —1, Parkes; 2, ’Enry Parkes; 3,
’Enery Parkes; 4, Sirenry Parkes.
One N.S.W. ex-Minister boasts that while
in office he never pitchforked a relative into a
Government billet. The little things he found for
young men from the Clarence—tidewaiterships,
&c. —no man would give to a relative. In a
democratic community it is better to placate a
dozen electors than one kinsman.
H. : “ The Bulletin evidently has a
sneaking penchant for Rosebery. As good as
they make them, but altogether in a false position,
for Liberalism has no standing in the world nowa-
days. It is Toryism or Radicalism, and Randy
Churchill takes the Disraeli-fancied combination
to his grave. Rosebery sits on a branch, and
saws it off. ”
Though Vic. Legislative Council rejected
the Land-tax on the ground that the gap between
income and outgo could easily be met by retrench-
ment, yet it rejected fully half of the retrench-
ment measures sent up the Assembly, and passed
the remainder with intense reluctance. The
Council has got so used to being a stumbling-block
that it blocks everything by sheer force of habit.
Spake he by authority or by surmise ?
Ex-Premier Dobson (Tas.) told the Eederal
Council that the Imperial Government would
appoint an Australian Judge to the Privy
Council’s Judicial Committee, provided Australia
footed the Bill. All the Tasmanian Dobsons are
lawyers, more or less ; but how can Henry know ?
Chief Justice Dobson, by the way, lately visited
England.
Mr. M‘Court’s difficulties and resignation
give rise to another effort to put Mr, Angus
Cameron into the N.S.W. Chairmanship of Com-
mittees. Whoever gets the post, it should cer-
tainly never be conferred on a man who, having
re-entered Parliament as an uncompromising
opponent of payment of members, has the front
to at once seek a paid Parliamentary appointment
of £7OO a year.
The second and decisive defeat of £. £.
Smith for Mornington means that young Victoria
is sick of the old boom-time family-party type of
legislator. The Liberals, hoping to bring about a
healthier political life, didn’t vote for Downward,
who is the dullest of Tories. They voted against
Smith. And they did this because they associate
Smith with “God save the Queen ” and log-roll-
ing, two habits that generally run in double-
harness.

Robert Stout, the alleged Radical of Maori-
land, says that, “from an economical point of
view, the Government advances to settlers will not
be of any real advantage to the settlers.” Stout
is a Democrat who, because he was lelt out of the
Democratic Ministry, is fast becoming a vicious
Tory and a cordial supporter of the foreign usurer.
The kind of Democrat who is only democratic
when he has a portfolio and a salary and is held
in reverence by the people, is a painful specimen.
Stout is that kind of Democrat. So was Griffith.
Stout is no longer stout. He is Fat.

The Board is entitled to conduct its own business.
Which means that the Metropolitan Board of
Works is superior to Parliament and beyond the
reach of the people. Anything is possible in a
Democracy —barring Fitz-Gibbon and the Argus.
Victoria might have been saved £2OOO a
year had the recent break-down in the cables
occurred a week sooner. It was an insinuation by
wire that Lord Brassey objected to the reduction
of the gubernatorial screw to .£5OOO that made
Turner ask Parliament to go back on itself and
add another =£2ooo instanter; and Parliament did
the wormy thing. After all, the insinuations
were deliberate Imperialistic lies, and Brassey
announced that he would be content with any pay,
but would have no haggling, so that the crawl was
uncalled-for. A movement for democratising the
cables is badly needed.
How is that among the many congratula-
tions tendered N.S.W. Railway-Commissioner
Eddy upon his reappointment, none has come
from the N.S.W. Socialist League ? Is it because
the Chief-Commissioner’s chief reviler has lately
fastened on to Socialism just as he has clung to
everything else likely to catch votes? Yet Eddy
has doii6 more for State Socialism than a thousand
of the dreamers. It is well for the true interests
of the State that the regime of intelligent railway
management has been made continuous. The
Eddy rule tends to keep the railway profits in the
hands of the people. Under “ private enterprise ”
the workers of N.S.W. would be made to sweat
blood.
N.S.W. Labor Electoral League’s new
dictum that, in all cases, the Government should
be its"own contractor is as visionary, in its way, as
the Single-tax is in its way. Some works are
better let by contract; others are better done by
day-labor; others are better left alone_ altogether.
The “way out ” for labor is to form itself into a
co-operative contractor, and then adhere to strict
“business principles.” Meanwhile, it may be
true that Government should let as few contracts
as possible, but to insist that it should let none is
to build up as hard and fast a quack doctrine as
that which declares that all countries should be
Freetrade. It is the expedient in business politics
that tells —the expedient, applied to a few under-
lying principles.
Nat Levi responded for the Vic. Legisla-
tive Council at the A.N.A. Rte luncheon (Melb.
Exhibition Building), the other Saturday.
Nathaniel had heard ringing cheers for Isaac
Isaacs’ attack upon the 48 land-owners who threw
out the Land Bill, so he put the best face he
could upon this job, and begged to thank the
country for applauding the Council’s noble con-
duct. Whereupon as much of the country as was
there present shouted “No ! ” The tough-hided
orator then fell back upon the fiction that 154,000
bread-winners are faithfully represented by the 48
land-owners, and he challenged any man to rise
and say that the Council didn’t reflect the senti-
ment of Victoria. The company immediately
rose, in spirit, as one man, and shouted some more
“No!” It next occurred to Nat Levi, M.L.C.,
that he was glad to be present on such a pleasing
occasion, after which he sat down amidst loud
applause.
In last Monday’s leading article, Sydney
Telegraph remarked
The cost of living being reduced, there is a propor-
tionate decrease in the cost of producing the things which
the squatter and the farmer have to buy. And so the
equalising process goes round. Take a few common
instances. A Sydney artisan who a few years back paid
£1 a week for his dwelling gets the same class of house
now for 12s. or 15s, Bread, which used to cost him from
3d. to 4d. per loaf, he gets at from 2d. to 2Jd. Heat, tea,
sugar, and all classes of food are similarly cheapened. If
he wants to take his family to Manly Beach, instead of
paying Is. 6d. for a return ticket, the trip costs only 6d.
Where he paid 3d. for his fare on a ’bus he now pays 2d.
These examples of the lessened cost of living in N.S.W.
cohid be carried throughout the whole catalogue of
human wants, which means that if the cash earnings of
the people have suffered, the intrinsic value of their labor
remains much the same.
The Telegraph here asserts that prices have
fallen all round during the last few years—which
covers the only period in her history when N.S.W.
was a protected province. How does this square
with the Telegraph’s other constant assertion
that Protection invariably Bends up prices all
round ?

The Tory press —especially the Argus,
S.M. Herald, and Australian Star— declaim
for ever, with a melancholy sound like the grind
of a broken grindstone, about the way the land-tax
and socialistic legislation are driving population
away from Maoriland. As a matter of fact,
Maoriland, in the six years before the land-tax
was imposed and the “ socialistic ” regime started,
lost 17,000 people toother countries by emigration,
and in the three years after the present nefarious
system came into force it gained 18,000 from other
countries.
The Argus, exposing the superfluity of
Socialism, and demonstrating the results of
“ natural ” progress during the last century :
The wealthiest man in Victoria uses no finer library
than the working man has access to ; he sits in no more
spacious or exquisitely-planned garden ; he rides no more
speedily in tram or coach, nor more luxuriously by train
than the working-man may easily do if he so chooses.
And yet the absurd workman often goes hungry
and ragged and bedless, and sees his wife sacri-
ficed to disease and want, and his children
dragged up in ignorance of the true joys of youth.
Only the Argus could conjure up such a Utopian
picture, with the assistance of a free library
(closed, in Melbourne, on the only day on which
the working-man can make use of it) and a
public garden. The Argus is incapable of seeing
that it uses the beauties of the few existing
socialistic institutions as arguments against the
extension of the system.

# • *
The Girl of Yarmouth’s prattle to a Mel-
bourne newspaper Jeannes included some bold
nonsense. “If I were in Eng and not the slight-
est notice would have been taken of my move-
ments,” said the lordling to the recording flunkey.
“ Why should I be held up to ridicule because I
chose to act for the benefit of a charity?” If Lord
Yarmouth were to do in the chief city of England
what he has done in the chief cities of Tasmania
and Victoria —viz., appear as the bright particular
theatrical star of two consecutive evenings—a lot
of notice would be taken of him. Also, if any 24-
year-old amateur, gilded or otherwise, were to
treat any community to a performance of himself
in two farces, one musical comedy, and sundry
variety turns, all in the same evening, he’d be held
up to ridicule whether he were appearing for
charity or purely on account of faith in his own
entertaining qualities. The young man has hurled
himself before the public here in the character of
a versatile actor. The best excuse he can now
make is that he uses his self-sought notoriety for
worthy purposes. ‘‘Every now and again,” he
said, “confidentially,” to the admiring grovel-
scribe, ‘T break out like this, get a crazy fit, and
want to act. Nobody takes any notice of me at
home ; why should they here?” Pay your money
for the privilege of looking at the young man, but
tike m notice of his antics, if you please. A
th atr) isn’t an asylum where visitors are expected
to ignore crazy fits.

The Bulletin finds that it was in error
in stating last week that Mayor Tucker, of Ade-
laide, was a bachelor and that Ids sister would act
as Mayoress. Mr. Tucker, The Bulletin now
heatfs, is in the happy position of a Benedict.
An announcement that Lady
Brassey will carry Victorian hearts by
storm has been made _as a matterof
course. The appointment of a new
Governor to any Australian province
is always followed by the same story
about his wife’s heaven-born capacity
for making “ society ” happy. Of yore
it was customary for a loyal press to
mention that the vice-regal system was
really founded o;i popular devotion to
the mistress of Guv’ment House. A
Governor’s lady, it was said, held the
Australian ends of the silken-bonds,
and devoted herself to the beautiful
task of drawing a community closer to
the mother country, &c. This awful
fiction p .ssad slowly out of print, as
one c unitess or knitrhtess after another
sneaked away to Britain for the benefit
of her health, and, perhaps, forgot to
return; but the advance notices re a
newcomer are still pretty sickening.
The odour of the old “ gag ” clings to
them. As soon as Brassey’s name
had been cabled The Bulletin could
discern the “exceptional charm ” of
Lady B.’s personality in the morning
air. _ „
# * *
The hard times are being felt
severely by the Salvation Army. At a
Victorian seaport the Army band made
a midnight patrol, playing at different
houses, in order to raise money to buy
chaff for the army horse. The attempt
succeeded, and on New Year’s Eve
another sortie was made, the object
being the liquidation of the gas-bill.
This was also a financial success, so
there is no knowing what will happen
next. One or two irate citizens arose
in their wrath to shoo away the mid-
night music, but as the best-looking
“lass” was always put forward to meet
the attack the “ remonstrance ” gener-
ally ended in a contribution to the funds.
The suicide of young Lloyd Burdekin
(writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent)
was one of the most remarkable, as it was appa-
rently one of the most motiveless, of any recorded
in recent times'. He had, of course, abundance of
money: he was steady; and had, so far as can be
learned, no female entanglements of any kind.
His parents, I believe, and even Mr. David
George, of the B.N.S.W. (brother of the late
Hugh George, of the Argus), who stood to him in
loco parentis, thought he was still at Oxford. Only
very recently young Ahck Hay, of Cooloongatta,
and Alfred Nathan went to Oxford to find him
out, and were surprised that they could get no
trace of him. They enquired of everybody who
would bo likely to know where he was, and what
he was doing, but it seemed as if he had already
passed out of existence. He had failed for his
preliminary exam, at the University ; and this, no
doubt, must have preyed on his mind, for, instead
of trying a second time, he seems to have come up
to London and taken rooms in Jermyn-street,
whence he migrated to Walton-on-Thames, where
he has for the last 18 months been leading the life
of a hermit, knowing nobody and seeing nobody but
his landlady. He does not seem to have had a
single acquaintance, and what the many local
professed friends of his family were doing not to
have enquired for him, or shown him some atten-
tion, passes understanding. No doubt he became
morbid, and the solitary life he led induced all
kinds of imaginings. For instance, he thought
that his failure in his exam, brought disgrace upon
him, and that people avoided him for that reason.
He fancied, too, that he had become repulsive-
looking, and that was another reason for his
solitary habits. He was a particularly sensitive
young fellow, and no doubt these delusions preyed
upon his mind. Otherwise there was no symptom
of aberration, and he was, like most of his people,
extremely shrewd. The letters which he wrote
on the night before he died might even be
said to be clever (except the childishly-worded
one to his parents), and that to his landlady.
But, if we look a little below the surface, we see
that these very letters prove that his mind was
unhinged, for so carefully planned were all his
preparations that if he had not disclosed his in-
tention nobody in the world would have thought
otherwise than that his death was the result of an
untoward accident. Never was calmer delibera-
tion shown. He had called his landlady to bring
up his breakfast so that she should see him at his
dressing-table in the act of selecting a neck-
tie or some other article of apparel. VVhilat
doing so, he must have seen the revolver in the
drawer, with a boot-lace entangled round the
trigger. What more natural than that he should
try to release it, and that the weapon exploded
with fatal effect? It was a clever idea. Had
he simply made his elaborate arrangements, all
would have pointed to an accident. But here the
want of balance of mind showed itself, and he
simply defeated his object by calmly sitting down
and placing on record what he had done. The
story is a sad one.

Dear Bulletin, —One Melb. P.M. has the
worst points of the would-be judicial humorist.
The other day a man pleaded for leniency on the
ground that he was a stranger, meaning that he
had not been before the court previously. “Two
months,” remarked his Worship—“that’s the
way we deal with strangers.’’ Again, the other
day, a prisoner asked for a remand, as he had a
widowed mother for whom he wished to make
provision if he had to go to gaol. “We can’t
help it if you have six,” was the brutal retort
from the Bench; “you should have thought of
that before committing such a cowardly assault.”
And this was before the evidence had been heard !
—M.G.
« * •
A Melb. correspondent to The Bulletin :
Editor Bulletin, — On Jan. 18 a lad of neglected ap-
pearance was brought before a Meltu P.M., charged with
indecent exposure. Though nothing else was known
against the lad's character, he was sentenced to a month s
gaol with a flogging. A few weeks previously a middle-
aged and well-to-do city trader was brought before the
same magistrate for a similar offence and simply fined.
In this offender’s case there were no extenuating circum-
stances whatever; he had repeated the offence, of
which he was convicted on the clearest evidence, in the
presence of women.
The Bulletin isn’t surprised at these things.
Indeed, it would marvel greatly were the facts
otherwise. The lash is not and never was in-
tended for the backs of well-to-do city traders.
It is designed—let The Bulletin say it for the
hundredth time—solely for the offenders in work-
man’s garb. No black-coated, long-hatted man
was ever, or ever will be, judicially lashed. Die
hangman’s whip is for John Smith, the son or the
husband of Mary Anne Smith, the washerwoman,
who has no fine feelings for Society to consider.
Society never ties up the well-tailored Reginald
de Smvthe, the cultured blackguard with the nice
scented sisters and the silver-haired mother whose
silk dress you can hear swish yards away. Some-
thing very sudden will happen one of these days
when a workman with a square jaw finds his son
lashed by the Govt, man-butcher for an offence
which young Mr. Reginald de Smythe has just
been allowed to expiate by a fine,
« * •
Why do some Sydney stipendiaries per-
mit the police to bring forward a list of convic-
tions in the middle of a hearing? The magis-
trates, of course, deny all prior knowledge of a
case, and say the conviction record has no influence
upon their decisions. If this is so, why do the
police secretly convey such information to the
magisterial ear? The other day a stipendiary
innocently told his court that he had seen a list of
convictions against a man charged before him.
Objections being raised by the defending solicitor,
it came out that the information was supplied by
the gaol authorities !

threatened, as one big city drapery firm intimates

once in three weeks as a recompense.

* * •
Mrs. Chas. Bright now appears as editor
of the Sydney magazine Cosmos, which prefaces
an article on Stevenson in the Jan. number with
the remark that “ his untimely death a few weeks
ago, at the early age of 44, makes his name the
most appropriate of all to grace the opening pages
of our magazine.” Convenient and thoughtful of
Stevenson, to be sure. Butchered to make a
Cosmos article 1 But the best items in the
number are the prize love-letters, in which
gushing young ladies (names and addresses
given in full) show how brilliantly they could
coo and woo an absent adorer. One of them (first
prize, £2 10s.) tells “My dearest” that “pen,
ink, and paper are delightful adjuncts to-day,
for I can write what I can scarcely tell when
every word is stopped by a caress.” Another
(second prize, ,£1) contradicts this—tells “My
own dearest” that pen, ink, and paper ruin the
language of love; and, after remarking twice that
“love-letters are a mistake,” winds up with
“Write to me, darling, write, write, write." It
will be seen that Cosmos is loyally keeping its
engagement to “permanently raise the standard
of Australian literature,” while, at the same time,
affording interesting pastime for the dear girls.

A typhoid patient with a temperature of
104° escaped from tiie Ballarat Hospital the other
night, made his way to Lake Wendouree, had a
swim, then, clad only in a shirt, coiled himself on
a bag in a boathouse, where he was found next
morning—on the high road to recovery.

A development in criminal tactics, which
Sydney police are carefully watching, consists in
what is called “ the game of substitution.” That
is, a member of the gang, not himself engaged in
the criminal exploit, puts himself in a compromis-
ing position soon after the crime is perpetrated
a id attracts to himself the attentions of the police,
confident that at the last moment he can prove his
innocence. It is thought by some that this was
Grenon’s relation to the Elizabeth Bay shooting
affray. The man who so acts is, of course, an
accomplice if the point can be proved, but this is
where the chief difficulty comes in.
* * •
A subscription ought to be raised for pre-
senting copies of Ibsen’s “ Enemy of Society ” to
the Scent Kilda Council. This honest body has
censured the Mayor of the puffumed seaside
snoburb for convening a public meeting, as requisi-
tioned, to call attention to the utterly insanitary
state of the place. Propputy, which has allowed
things to go to the dogs to save the rates, is
indignant that it should be known that the conse-
quence has been that Scent Kilda has become
unfit to live in. It is precisely the old story
again. The Mayor is Ibsen’s Dr. Stockman, and
the Council is the “damned compact majority.”

In that North Shore (Sydney) case the
man has just experienced a narrow escape from a
common risk. The girl was weak first and sorry
and hysterical afterwards. Queerly enough, this
particular woman was not a hardened liar, and,
under cross - examination, candidly and self-
damningly owned up to a voluntary liaison.
Which led to the accused’s discharge, but only after
he had been four days in gaol charged with a
neck-stretching offence.

“How did the Salvationists raise the
.£5OOO required as a deposit on the purchase of the
Y.M.C.A.’s Melb. premises?” The back of it
was broken by a penny tax—to purchase, or
build, a head-quarters—on every collection made
during about five years past.
‘We have a God who takes a special
interest in the Salvation Army,” says the last
War Cry. This, mind you, is not Dagon, or
Baal, or Tophet, or any of the gods of Hamath
and Arphad, or the gods of Sepharvaim, and very
evidently it is not the angel aloft that looks after
poor Jack Y.M.C.A., whose palace the Army has
just bought over the Melb. Young Men’s heads.

# # •
Another rude shock to “reverence” in
Australia. An old Cardinal’s hat was treasured
at Melb. Cathedral, on account of the mystery
attaching to it. Nobody knew how it came there.
It had been there longer than the oldest habitue
could remember, and was beginning to possess the
sanctity of a relic, when, one day, an old press-
man visited the place, and was shown the myste-
rious Cardinal’s hat. He solved the mystery in one
act. Many years ago he had attended a ceremony
commemorating the death of an eminent Cardinal,
at which a dummy coffin was employed, and a
Cardinal’s hat was obtained from a Melb. tailor
to put upon the coffin. The treasured hat was the
one the tailor had made !
* * *
Christchurch (M.L.) female saints have
out-gimleted their sisters in other cities. Their
newest move is to hide in dark corners near “ gay”
houses, watch those who enter, and send the
names of such as they can discover to their em-
ployers. Their very first victim was the leading
musician of a city church. The particulars of his
iniquity were forwarded to the high priest, and
ho was promptly sacked. Action for damages is
being taken in the matter.

It was after the third test-match had been
won and lost, in Adelaide, that the eyes of the
cricketing world were directed upon A. E. Trott.
The Englishmen before this had taken note of
him, and Peel, particularly, had spoken to a
Bulletin interviewer in marked praise of him as
a colt. But suddenly in the test-match the colt
eclipsed all records ; he scored 38 (not out) in the
first innings, 72 (not out) in the second, and he
sent ruin into the ranks of the English in their
second innings by dismissing eight of them for 43.
And no one was more surprised than the colt him-
self, and no one realises more than he does how
much he excelled himself by that great perform-
ance. He says he was never in better trim than he
was then; he was as cool as if playing an ordinary
club match ; the hard wicket suited him perfectly;
heoften broke back six inches. “A cricketer from
babyhood,” people will naturally say, of this fine
young athlete, 21 years old, 12st. 91b. in weight,
sft. lOin. in height. He is moved by the cricket
spirit; he would rather field for the opposition
side than sit still and look on. Besides, he has
never gone in for any other sport, except base-
ball—in the winter, mind ! and occasional
skittles.’ (There are 10 baseball clubs in Mel-
bourne 'how). Moreover, other sports don’t like
him; his first and last attempt at riding ended in
his being thrown against a tree, and, because he
is only a moderate swimmer, and was a little too
venturesome the other day, at Coogee, an
unusually heavy breaker caught him in its
embraces, and nearly mauled the life out
of him upon the hard and slippery rocks.
Facts are facts ; and as Trott has never indulged
in fencing, boxing, wrestling, rowing, sailing,
shooting, running, football, bicycling, dumb-bells,
or clubs, and as he is an Australian and must,
therefore, have a sport, and as he has put up a
cricket record at the age of 21, he has most pro-
bably been “a cricketer from babyhood.” But,
on the contrary, he most emphatically says that
he was made into a cricketer after he was 16 years
old. Till then he did not play in the real sense,
for casual attempts resulting in ducks’-eggs do
not constitute cricket. But five years ago Barrett,
of Melbourne, reasoned from analogy that as H.
Trott was a first-class cricketer, and brother to
A. B. Trott, it would be justifiable to experiment
with the younger brother and try to evoke
the cricket that was probably iii him. So
Barrett brought him to the South Melbourne
nets of an afternoon and taught him how
to break the ball, and encouraged him
wisely. The youngster worked with a will ;
bowling as he does now, three or four hours at a
stretch, and progressing favorably. Three years
ago he left South for East Melbourne, and is now
employed by that club as a professional cricketer,
and as a cricketer only. Last season his batting
average for his club was 19 runs an innings, his
bowling analysis 10 runs per wicket. Prior to his
sensational performance in the third test-match,
his highest score was 63, made twice; the last time
being in one match of this season, East Melbourne
v. Fitzroy. Two months ago, playing with his
club against a Mornington 15 he secured 14
wickets for 23. His debut in interprovincial
cricket was against Adelaide, and affords a con-
trast to his last big feat : Ist innings, caught
and bowled Giffen, 0; 2nd innings, 0 not out.
When batting, his right foot is on a level with his
bat at “block;” he has no pet bat of his own,
but he never plays with a heavy one. Bowling,
he runs about eight or nine yards before deliver-
ing. To bowl fast, he holds the ball entirely witli
his first two fingers and thumb, the ball’s seam
being equidistant (about half an inch, at most)
from each finger. To bowl his off-break, he gels
all the twist on with his forefinger, which, through
incessant bowling, is most markedly stronger and
stouter than the corresponding finger of the left
hand : he grips the ball so that the third and
fourth fingers act as a rest under it; the forefinger
comes over the ball until the nail ii opposite the
ball of the thumb; the thumb itself is at right angles
to the longest phalange of the forefinger and its
joint rests thereon, so that the upper part of the
thumb stands up prominently and is, in fact, the
plainest of signals to a batsman, who can thus see
the off-break coming before ever the ball leaves
his hand. This defect was pointed out to him by
Peel, but Trott has learnt to bowl that way and
he cannot alter it. It is a queer method, peculiar
to him as a bowler. He has no nerves, and has
never been to a doctor in his life. He is a teeto-
taller, but an habitual cigarette-smoker.
Treas. Holder (S.A.) contemplates a visit
to the Northern Territory, returning overland
with Griffiths, M.P., a la Kintore ; but S.A.
politicians are making a hubbub at this proposed
waste of public money. S.A. has not forgotten
the trip made by Johnson, when Minister for the
N.T., which ended in smoke; and Playford’s ex-
pensive trip of a few years ago. Holder, like the
predecessors named, is in ill-health, and needs a
holiday.

My Dkar Moorabinda—
At Admiral Bridge’s inaugural grass-party at
Admiralty House, the music was delicious, and
the afternoon a success from truck to keel-
son. i’lie Admiral makes a most intelligent
host. He glides about, and has a word in season
for everybody, or, at least, for all those people
who dofi’t turn in their toes and look as if they
got in through a gap in the fence whenever their
host makes a tack in their direction. Which
somehow reminds, me of a well-known “ society ”
woman ;(she was a beginner then) whom Gov.
Carington once strolled up to with the cruelly-kind
intention of having a little yarn. And, when H.
E. drew near, ferociously twisting his moustache,
the poor woman begun wildly diving into a back
pocket, from whence she produced and conspicu-
ously displayed her invitation-card. She evidently
thought the Baron was about to indict her for
trespass. 1
In one way, it wouldn’t be a bad idea if people
had to display their tickets-of-leave at these big
official shivoos, “ for their own protection and
ours,” as the shopkeepers say, as there are many
social trespassers who go poaching after pleasure.
I can onl£ recall one Australasian instance when
a whole flight of these interlopers got bagged, and
that transpired some time ago in a leading Maori-
land city, wher. the wives of the Ministry then in
office gave a big official ball, to which everybody
with a ball-dress or a dress-suit got an elaborate
invitation.’ S unething postponed the hop for a
week or so, however, and due notice was given to
the invited guests—a privilege in which the unin-
vited ditto did not share. You may guess the
sequel. They all turned up on the first date fixed
in a run of hired cabs, only to beat a confused
retreat five minutes afterwards. That little ex-
perience pretty well scotched the mob-nuisance in
Maoriland.
I was idly speculating, the other day, on the
unearthly dearth of engagements—mere engage-
ments that are, after all, invalid things, like un-
signed IO U’s or blank cheques. Cast your eye over
the marriage-market and you’ll find a most un-
promising glut of girls, and no reason for it, either,
that I can see. Certainly one very wealthy man
won’t allow his eldest daughter to encourage any-
body but an : Australian suitor, so her long adorn-
ment of the parental stem is partially explained,
seeing that her choice is limited to one continent
—but what of the gilded rest, who have the whole
earth upon which to use their powers of attrac-
tion ?
Whether the falling-off in engagements (I do
not clamor for more marriages—only let us have
tome guarantee, from time to time, of the matri-
monial good faith of Australian raarnageables)
argues that the New Woman is taking the place of
the old and repudiating the joys of double bliss,
is a nut for chaperones to crack, but it certainly
is odd that, although the “ society ” newspapers
reek with long accounts of routs and kettledrums,
and the fashions are followed to the last gasp,
yet such thrilling announcements as “ the wed-
ding of Miss Blank with Mr. Asterisk will be
solemnised on the onetieth instant,” rarely occur
to sustain one’s interests in the dovecotes.
Certainly women are like the goods in the shop-
windowsi which go so stale that the pedestrians
who pass every day rarely look at them, even
carelessly, at last. Like prophets, women prosper
best afar off. Only think 1 One of Sydney’s
prettiest girls married an Englishman at ’Ome the
other day. A musical Sydneyite went abroad to
study, and married en route. A banker’s daughter
took a Continental tour —met a man whom she
had known all her life in Australia, but under
foreign skies he took on a different aspect and
they married. I could multiply instances to
boring-point. Therefore, any maiden who feels a
little dog’s-eared in her native place is urgently
advised to pack up and flee to some happy isle
where the women are as unlike her own type as
possible—though I don’t mean by that to en-
courage a strawberry-blonde to make a bee-line
for Fiji.
I have been much entertained lately by the
spiritual airs and graces that the congregation of
a very fashionable Anglican church (under V.R.
patronage) lias seen fit to assume ever since its
“ priest ” cast off a fat vineyard and many hun-
dreds per annum in order to toil in a smaller field
for little more than one-fifth of the sum it
willingly contributed. It certainly is a charming
surprise to find a priest obeying a call embodying
such a startling reduction in perks, and his late
flock, though it mourns his loss, yet cannot help
feeling pardonably proud of having dry-nursed so
good a man. At the same time, it ought to be
remembered that it is frequently better business
to serve in Heaven than to reign in the rival shop.
I have a deep conviction that all imported
Anglican parsons in Australia (I may dilate
Ladies and gentlemen, remember that to
prevent a cold is much better than to cure it, and
by using the sanitary toilet requisite, “ Amykos,”
according to the advices on the circular wrapped
round the neck of the bottle, you will not alone
guard yourself against a cold, but find what a plea-
sant -Mid refreshing taste Amykos has compared
with other preparations in the market. On the
continent it is universally used by all who esteem
cleanliness Sold by all chemists, etc., at Is. 6d,
per bottle.
freely on this topic, you see, because no clergyman
ever dreams of reading The Bulletin) have a
rooted belief that, if they are good, by-and-by a
critical Providence will land them into a tidy little
job at 'Ome. An English cure is to the average
“kawlonial” incumbent what Paris is to an
American belle —a lovely place, where wealthy
spinsters never cease from troubling with crewel-
work braces, and the rude, sceptical Australian is
at rest. A place, furthermore, where every fad
gets a following of idle, overfed women, with a
spiritual yearning to suit all requirements, and
where the “ priest ” moves in the dim religious
light of tapers, and hasn’t to face a great glaring
sun, which shows up at the same moment the
powder on the choir-girls’ noses and the crow’s-
feet on the Lord s anointed. Yes, England is un-
doubtedly their ’Ome.
The terrible weather spoilt the test cricket-
match on the Sydney Association Ground
for the second day (Saturday), though a good
sized crowd turned up on Friday. Cricketers
must be singularly free from superstition—they
always start on that much-suspected day. Women
in what I call cosmea colors made the Members’
look like a garden from a little way off; there
was band music, too, a luxury much missed
at the other match, and the riotous display of flags
was as if every known nation had sent its particu-
ar banner to the wash and they were all drying
.ogether atop of the judge’s stand.
Talking about cricket—the übiquitous qualities
possessed by the relics of many saints are as
naught beside the übiquity of the toss-up shilling
which Giffen threw among the crowd, with the
somewhat fatalistic remark: “I sha’n’t want it
any more,” when Stoddart won the toss. I have
personally encountered and roundaboutly heard
tell of fully a dozen youths and one “lydy ” who
all possessed the identical shilling which Giffen
threw among the crowd. As probably not even
Mr. G. himself would recognise that nimble
shilling again, nobody is likely to be bowled out
about it; but in the moral interests of a young
and thoughtless nation, the next hero who casts
his coin upon the waters really ought to change it
into 48 farthings.
I have received from the composer, Miss Sylvia
Blackstone, a rather pretty gavotte—the “ Green-
oaks” —dedicated to Mrs. Hutton. The gavotte
is neatly and naturally written, and, without
being startingly original, is a most promising
composition. It is also easy to play.
As Victoria’s brand-new Governor-elect, Lord
Sunbeam Brassey, starts for Melbourne in Sep-
tember, he should just be in time to decorate
Flemington lawn on Cup-day. The most magni-
ficent rumors are afloat concerning the suite, and
Melbourne belledom must be tiptoe with expecta-
tion. The (youngest) son of a marquis and a real
earl are only a few 'Of the splendid creatures
whom rumor has attached to Lord Brassoy’s en-
tourage—on paper, at least. Let us pray that
when they turn up they will not be the same old
jerry-built Johnnies, very much gone at the
knees and ejaculating a feeble “ Haw ” at long
intervals.
I can’t help thinking that Victoria has occa-
sion to be deeply grateful that it is Lady Brassey
No. 2 and not her ladyship No. 1, who is fated to
preside over the biggest Government House in
Australia. The Lady Brassey we used to know
was one of the faddiest of women, and would have
kept Cabbage-garden Society “on the jump,”
what with ambulance-lectures, sanitary talks and
all sorts of improving pow-wow. The imminent
Lady B. is decidedly pretty, judging from her
photos., and looks like a woman to whom a
neatly-curled taupe was of far more importance
than a mustard-poultice. Though there is nothing
like a mustard-poultice (thank goodness !) —still,
too keen an interest in it is not devoutly to be
wished in the leader of a more or less frivolling
society. It’s like making a busy-bee leader to a
team of butterflies to have a Woman with a Mission
at Government House.
Apropos of Governors, it strikes me as being a
decided reflection on Eton and Oxford that so
many Australian professors of Shakespeare and
the musical-glasses now go about proudly display-
ing testimonials from his Excellency testifying to
the great improvement his lordship has made in
his study of the rule of thumb under Professor
Blank’s able guidance, and things like that—not
thanking the Dominie on behalf of the vice-regal
nursery, mind you, but on His Ex.’s own! One
Sydney teacher of French flourishes a testimonial
from Lord Jersey, and in a long circular makes
the somewhat astonishing statement that—“ His
Excellency Lord Jersey after a few months’ lessons
was able to speak with fluency,” &c. But, imagine
an earl with the run of the “’Varsities” only
learning his parley-voo in Sydney ! It seems
absolutely incredible. But it throws a new
light on vice-regal sufferings in the beastly
—no, I mean highly-enlightened—colonies. Many
and many a time, 1 expect, when a Gover-
nor has seemed to cut up rusty and refused
at the eleventh hour to open a new town pump or
something, the real reason of his absence was
because he was “ kept in ” to write out his verbs !
I sometimes think we would all like to lie down
in our little graves with our white hands folded
on our breasts and florins on our eyelids if it
wasn’t for the dread of that awful something after
death - the obituary - notice. In a Southern
“society” paper a gilt-edged pen-lady embalms
the demise of poor, dear Mrs. Dash at the early
age of 29. She was just going to marry again,
and her untimely death “is said to have been due
to eating unripe fruit.” •
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.
