Editor Bulletin. —Sir, —I have shown to
a personal friend of yours the paragraph in The
Bulletin of the 2nd February, detailing an
imaginary visit paid by me to Mr. Wallis’ office.
He assures me that you worM scorn to malign a
woman and would despise the informer who led
you to editorially endorse such a false statement
as the one taken objection to. I pledge you my
word that there is not an atom of truth in the
paragraph, and that I have never been in Mr.
Wallis’ office.—Believe me, sincerely yours, Annie
Mellon, “Wahroonga,” Waverley Road, Wool-
lahra. Feb. 4, ’95.
The camel has come to stay with a ven-
geance in Westralia, and the local papers teem
with advertisements of mobs to arrive or on the
spot. There is unusual activity in the camel
market, and the port of Fremantle has lately
been very busy disembarking hundreds of these
ungainly" but useful beasts. Quite the finest lob
of camels that have yet reached Westralia from
India are 300 imported by Sultan Ras Mahomed,
of Coolgardie, which are now on sale close to
Perth and are rapidly being snapped up. Captain
C. D. Saunders is the manager for Raz Mahomed,
and is at present resident in Perth for the purpose
of disposing of the camels to diggers and prospec-
tors about to trv their luok.
SPRTING NOTIONS.
In the fourth test-matoh Stoddart won the toss,
and ssnt Australia in on a putty wicket; one of the worst
Ned Gregory ever saw. At the start this wicket was red-
hot and Richardson terrific. Hurling the ball with all his
force, he often broke back over a foot. These fast break-
backs paralysed the Australians; six of the front-flight
batsmen went down for 51. Iredale was caught and
bowled first ball. Graham was told by Miss Gregory —no
mean authority, by the way—to go in and hit; Richard-
son’s first ball nearly went over the fence, the second flew
to the boundary ; Graham knocked Richardson off. Obey-
ing a girl he bore a charmed life ; the English tried to
catch him out five times and failed. Had Graham fallen
at the first chance, the wicket, improving every moment,
would have enabled the English to make the stand that
was afterwards made by Graham, Trott, and Turner.
Young Trott astonished everyone by driving up 85, not
out; he, too, had been told “to hit” by Miss Gregory.
This Australian girl won the fourth test-match. The
innings closed for 284-Graham and Trott making
190. There was no play on Saturday, the second day,
for the rain poured so that it promised a drought in
Heaven. And on Monday, though the morning was fine,
with a bright sun and drying wind, experts, after examin-
ing the wicket, prophesied a one-innings defeat for the
Englishmen.
No team could look for victory on that wicket
against Turner, Giffen and the two Trotts. But the
English, who expected to make 150 in their first innings,
utterly collapsed. Brown went in third wiuket down,
and made top score —20, not out ; Briggs got 11; Stoddart
and Ward were next with 7 ; the total score made off the
bat was only 53. It was not the wncket that did all this ;
the Australian bowling and fielding were splendid ; Giffen,
bowling, caught the last man, Richardson, with his left
hand as by a miracle; Richardson stood still, staring at
this feat. Giffen brought the ball with him into the
pavilion ; one enthusiast offered him £5 for it. The Aus-
tralian party-feeling was rampant; there were many men
who w'aited with their hands ready to clap every English
mistake.
• ••♦**
Peel and Briggs went out with Ned Gregory to
inspect the wicket, and to decide whether a heavy or
light roller was to go upon it; the wicket, which was like
a sponge, might, if too heavily rolled, bring the water to
the surface. The second innings of the English opened
ominously. Brown, the not-out hero of the first, came
out and faced Giffen. Fourth ball his stumps were down ;
it was not the wicket that bowled that yorker. The
wicket was improving ; but the English were demoralised,
cowed by the deadly deliveries of Turner and Giffen, and
the conviction that to hit a ball up was to be caught.
Turner bowled too well, breaking the ball too much
and missing the stumps. He and Giffen placed their
field in daring defiance of the reputation of
the English batsmen ; young Trott stood slip,
the elder Trott point; these were the only men
to stop cuts, but the English never made a cut,
and out of a total of 59 off the bat only 4 were carpet-
drives, all singles ; Ford made 3of these. The names of
Brown, Stoddart, and M‘Laren were quickly hoisted for
0 each ; three wickets were down for 5 ; five wickets (four
of w hich totalled 0) fell for 11 Splendid as was the bowd-
ing, its merit was neutralised by the helping wicket; but
there was only admiration for the Australian fielding. The
Australians missed no possible chance, no finer fielding
was ever seen -the English called it an exhibition. Brock-
well made 17, Ford 11, Richardson 10 (not out). The
second innings closed in less than two hours, and the
English were defeated by an innings and 147 runs.
• * ♦ •
Lopkwood is a great admirer of George Giffen.
“ 1 think the test-matches would be one-sided affairs for
the Englishmen if George Giffen were not against us,
bowling with his unerring length and straightness, and
then going in after to make a big score.”
* * * *
One or two of the English are putting Giffen,
as a bowler, before Turner. “ Turner is not the man he
was.”
* * * *
Graham was put into the Australian Eleven in
blank defiance of public opinion, and when six Australian
wickets fell for 61 the aggrieved public began to feel that
the sth test-match had been won for the Englishmen,
partly by the Australian Selection Committee, and partly
by the treacherous coin which Giffen lost the toss to
Stoddart with, and threw away among the crowd dis-
gustedly. And Graham made 105 ! and gave Australia a
first-class thrill.
• * * *
Patrons of tile leg-stroke—Gregory, Moses,
Stoddart, Iredale, Shrewsbury, Lockwood. More leg-
strokes are made in Australia than in England : the ball
there often jumps too much.
• * * *
A side-light on England v. Australia : Usual
county cricket in England means playing matches 30, 40,
or 50 days running, exclusive of Sundays. Ordinary Aus-
tralian cricket consists of playing matches on Saturday
afternoons, and many an Australian batsman has kept
himself in form, tempting Providence and the laws of
digestion, by batting for a quarter of an hour in the heat
of the day, after bolting down his lunch. Often in Eng-
land a cricketer is almost glad to stand out of a county
match to get three or four days’ spell.
♦ ♦ • •
When Adelaide made the winning hit against
the Englishmen, the ball went among the crowd, and was
returned by a boy to Lockwood. Lockwood didn’t keep
it as a treasure ; he knocked the cover off it in practice.
« • * •
The Englishmen, admitting that they played
badly in the Adelaide test-match, say that it was chiefly
through playing too carefully “ for keeps.”
* ♦ ♦ ♦
Australian crowds are very partial, and some
of the barrackers (to their disgrace) insult the English-
men in the outfield. “ The crowd ought to be as generous-
minded as the Australian cricketers,” the Englishmen
* ay ’ *
Says Lockwood : “ Turner has bad luck as a
bowler, but, all the same, the Englishmen miss many more
catches in the outfield than the Australians do., I think
we are better in ground-fielding, though.”
* » • •
A bottle of soda bursting made it impossible for
Lockwood to bat in the last test-match, and the English-
men accordingly batted one man short. The glass cut his
hand so badly that it chipped out a piece of bone.
♦
By coming to Australia, the Englishmen get
three cricket seasons in succession. Several of them say
that they look forward to the next English winter as a
welcome rest.
* ♦ ♦ ♦
Turner, on being twitted by an old friend with his
seeming inability to score in some recent matches, can-
didly admitted his batting to be poor, but added that it
might he better were he given a more favorable place.
Being sent in about eighth or ninth man completely dis-
heartened him, and he didn’t care who knew it. Although
on the selection committee, he has to bow before the
captain for the time being, and it is an open secret that
he rather chafes against this. But his services in the last
test-match are acknowledged.
* * • •
Editor Bulletin,—lt is to be regretted that
De Maus, the Christchurch crack, could not have been
included in one of the test-matches. Although only 22
years of age he has been regarded the champion bat of
Maoriland for the past four or five years ; having made
highest aggregate of the 22 in most of the interprovincial
matches. For five completed innings against N.S.W.
teams which have visited Maoriland, he has an average
of 74. He is a successful bowler on sticky, but an expen-
sive one on dry wickets, and scores very rapidly off fast
bowling. In an emergency he is invaluable, being one of
the coolest bats that ever faced a bowler.— Phormi.

second-class match played on Adeluid ‘ Oval, Jan. 25.

against Wistow (22)—Frank Von Doussa took 11 wicket*

The veteran Robert Orr finds the Ballarat sport-
ing crowd so lukewarm that he has relinquished the idea
of a small pack of hounds to keep the local Hunt Club
alive. Said club is about to compound with its creditors
for 7s. in the £, without reference to the big amount
owing to its hard-working secretary. The kennels have
again been sold, and are now to be used as a piggery.
The pride of the Russells, Chirnsides, Wilsons and Main-
folds replaced by beastly swine 1

applied for before issue of this prospectus,
early application on the part of intending sub-
scribers is necessary,
PROSPECTUS
OF
Harris’s Reward Gold Mine No Liability,
Mount Jukes, West Tasmania,
CAPITAL, .£24,000, in 48,000 Shares of 10s. each,
whereof 24,000 shares fully paid up are reserved
in part payment of the vendors, and the remain-
ing 24,000 shares are offered at
Is. 6d. per share on application,
2s. 6d. per share on allotment.
Calls, if required, not to exceed 3d. per share.
In addition to the shares above mentioned, tha
vendor receives ,£3600 in cash on transfer of tha
property, out of which he pays the brokerage on
the placing of shares, and a further sun* of .£lOOO
is to be paid him out of the first proceeds of the
mine, at the rate of one-third obtained from the
gold from each crushing.
The advertising and legal expenses, fixed at a
sum of .£75, will be paid by the company.
The company may be registered as soon as
20,000 shares have been applied for.

BROKERS.
Melbourne —
Tasmania—Hobart, Bayley and Walch ; Laun-
ceston, E. Gaunt; Zeehan,
This company is formed to acquire and work
that wonderfully auriferous discovery at Mount
Jukes (12 miles from Mount Lyell), Tasmania,
known as Harris’s Reward Claims, comprising in
the claims an area of 220 acres or thereabouts.
This discovery is so well-known that it is un-
necessary to enter into a lengthened description of
it, but it may be stated that it consists of an
auriferous lode about Bft. wide of hard blue lami-
nated quartz, carrying a large percentage of galena
and pyrites, and showing gold all over freely ;
estimated to yield 4oz. per ton. The lode, which
is a true north and south one, outcrops on the sur-
face, and has been traced for a distance of about
14 chains. About 300 tons of the rich ore which is
in sight can be quarried.
A small specimen assayed at the local Mines
office yielded loz. gold and 3oz. silver (from the
specimen, not per ton), and the whole ore body is
estimated, as above stated, to yield soz. of gold
j er ton.
Ore to keep a large crushing plant permanently
employed can be mined forthwith without expen-
diture on works, whilst there is an abundant
supply of both water and timber, and a battery of
10 heads close to the claim can te had at a small
price.
There is no doubt that the rich alluvial gold
worked in the immediate neighbourhood of this
claim was derived from this line of reef.
As an evidence of his belief in the mine, the
vendor is content to take a large portion of the
purchase money out of the proceeds of the mine.
If no allotment, the purchase money will be re-
turned in full; in the case of allotted shares, if
the allotment money is not paid within 10 days of
allotment the allotment may be cancelled, and the
application deposit forfeited to the company.
Reports, specimens and plans will be oil view at
the company’s offices, in Melbourne, in a few
days, and a careful inspection of these is confi-
dently invited.

Bad biz. is the rule in Melbourne, and nearly
all firms are now paying all travellers by commission,
thereby increasing embezzlement, as men cannot live on
air when things are bad. Even lawyers are adopting this
system. Some of their clerks receive no pay, but,
to make the office look full, are paid commission on
the biz. they bring in. Apropos, the Vio. Law Institute
promises to be very busy at the end of the long vacation,
Feb. 1.
• ♦ * ♦
A remittance-man wished a bill done ; the bill-
shaver found representations 0.k., and, to keep the biz.
from straying, he insisted on the borrower having his
letters addressed to him, and on the right of opening the
one containing the remittance. This was agreed to ; the
consequence is that a bill is always current. The remit-
tance-man finds it so convenient to be able to run in and
getapound when he is short. And he is always short.
The shaver has lived on this one transaction alone for the
last eighteen months.

business.

Two letters—one from Sweden and the other
from Germany-received in Sydney during last week, so
spoke of the depression and difficulty of getting in money
in those countries that when translated the words would
be taken as an exact description of the commercial and
industrial omdition of Australia.
• * * ♦
Of the precious metals (manufactured) slyly
acquired up North a great proportion finds its way into
the hands of a dear old gentleman who prides him-
self on being frequently mistaken for a clergyman.
Another of the old gentleman's eccentricities is to boil
down immediately he receives them the choicest speci-
mens of the goldsmith’s art, no matter how valuable the
workmanship may be.
♦* * *
The Maori land Government Loan Department
is now fairly started, and in 10 years or so some of the
pessimists of to-day will have a fair chance to decide
whether the scheme is a failure or not. So tar as present
appearances go the situation is something like this:
There are £30,000,000 lent on real estate mortgages in
Maoiiland, and the average interest is 7 per cent., ora
little more, therefore the community pays £2,100,000 a
year, and still owes the same old £30,000,000, which never
shifts one iota. Under the Government scheme borrowers
will pay 5 per cent, interest, and 1 per cent, towards
redemption of the principal, which will thereby be
extinguished in 36 years. Therefore, if the whole mort-
yaje business of the country can be gradually absorbed
by'the State, or—what is the same thing—if the private
lender can be forced down to the State’s terras, the
country will pay £1,800,000 a year for principal and in-
terest instead of £2,100,000 for interest alone, and in 36
years the saving to the community will he a small trifle of
£10,000,000. Tub Bulletin has remarked the same thing
before, but out of deference to a slow and thick-headed
community which absorbs new ideas very gradually it
hereby states it all qver again, to impress upon Australians
the necessity for trying a similar experiment.
Just now applications for loans are pouring in
on the Department faster than they can be dealt with.
This effectually extinguishes the Tory assertion that the
country doesn’t want the new system, isn’t interested in
it, and feels that it is much better off without it. To the
other class of pessimists wdro allege that the Department
will go broke by-and-bye through making excessive
advances which the security won’t be suffh ient to cover
when the inevitable depression comes along, it is only
necessary to point out that the State is going to take
much less risk in the matter than the average private
lender. The ordinary Corporation lends £lOOO on land
worth £l5OO, and when a slump cmnes, say ten years
hence, and the land is only worth £9OO, a good part < I
its principal is gone. The State insist son get ling back its
principal by instalments, and when the same slump
arrives its £lOOO loan is reduced to about £877, and it is
still covered. Moreover, in bad times a man with a £lOOO
mortgage at 7 per cent, often throws up the game, while
the one with a similar liability at 5 per cent, pulls
through. Altogether the State Loan Department will be
the safest, as well as the cheapest, money-lending concern
in Maoriland. Meanwhile, many private lenders have
reduced their rates from 7 or 7J to 5 per cent, in order to
compete with the Givernment office, and already the
mortgagor feels considerably better.

The practice of forwarding Melb. bankrupt
hardware and other stocks to Sydney for sale—coming as
it does on top of last year’s bad business-is regarded as
calculated to drive a host of small Sydney shopkeepers
into bankruptcy.
* • * *
A “good” firm of accountants has lost the
periodical audit of a large firm whose head office is in
Europe. The latter firm has a capable book keeper, who
was surprised at the lightning rapidity of the audits. To
lest their value, a thousand-pounds error was purposely
made. It pissed the auditors successfully, the head office
was apprised of the test, and received the usual report
from the auditing firm, whose services were at once dis-
pensed with.
r * * * »
The Vic. annual return of insolvencies, &c., for
’94, omits to state the amount realised from assets,
amount distributed to creditors, amount mopped up in
ex.’s. It makes no mention of any officials who have gone
bung, having embezzled moneys belonging to estates in
their care ; no mention of any official fr< m whose office no
estate ever paid a div. The return even ignores the case
of the official, who, having “blown” the trust-funds,
called a meeting of his creditors, who were chiefly the
creditors of the estates in his hands.

A Vic. bowling club had a green, its private
property. During the boom a good price was offered for
it, which it accepted after securing, through its
member a grant (for nix) of a good slice of a public park
for a fresh green. Nothing verdant about that club com-
mittee.

A sweet little creature had her place nicely fur-
nished by a Northern time-payment firm, which now
wishes, in face of the arrears, to take the goods back. The
house-agent is an admirer of the sex, and had the house
vacant for 12 months prior to Flossie taking it, yet the
T.P. firm is met with a claim for rent for that term, plus
the time Flossie has been in, and is confronted with a
proper agreement bearing correct dates. Flossie’s domes-
tic arrangements will remain undisturbed. Thus do the
T.P. people gain another wrinkle.
Ed. Bulletin,—From the Adelaide Observer,
22nd. Dec., '94 :—“ Coolgardie, December 17.—Mr. W. B.
Wilson has returned from a visit to the Menziea line of
country, flu had a novel experience when crossing the
lake beyond the Ninety Mile. A thundershower had
fallen just before they reached the lake, which is seven
miles across. The salt water lies a foot or two below the
surface. The rain had quickly converted the top of the
lake into a slippery mass on which the horses could
scarcely keep their feet, and all the party had to walk the
whole distance. They covered most of the seven m.'ea
skating on the perfectly smooth surface of the lake.
The lake referred to is like all the other salt lakes here, ,
parched in dry weather and intensely boggy after a
shower. I crossed the above lake half an hour before
Wilson’s party, and it took both men and camels all their
time to lift one foot out of the mud before placing the
other one down. Where the skating came in 1 fail to
understand.— Prospector.
* » * •
If it were sought to oust the cement-workers at
Hannan’s from their ground on the plea that cement is
not alluvial hut reef, that pha would emphatically not do;
for cement isn’t reef, but alluvial pure and simple. The
people who seek to oust the cement-workers, and mono-
polise their ground, claim that the reeling-leases issued for
the ground having been approved, the present cement-
workers must quit—that, in fact, it is too late for the
working miners to argue the matter. The issue of reef-
ing-leases for alluvial gi ound shouldn’t hold good under
any circumstances, and Tub Bulletin is glad to see a
wire stating that the alluvial men are to retain possession.

Wilson is prominently associated, including the Trenton
at Cue, Heffernan’s find at Mt. Margaret, and two or
t iree others at CoMgardie. Wilson is the boss W.A.
prospector, and, so far, his money lias not been covered by
W.A. gold. Next to themselves, Coolgardie men wish
W.R.W. good luck.

The Mining Registrars of Vic. quote the ’94
gold-yields of their respective districts as follows:
Ballarat .. .. 194,819
Beechworth ~ .. 95,996
Bendigo .. .. 159,711
Mary boro’ .. .. 64,338
Castlemaine .. .. 64,886
Ararat .. .. 27,590
Gippsland .. .. 66,289
673,679
The total for the province shows an increase on a similar
estimate for ’93 of 25530 z. The districts showing a
decrease are: Ballarat, 13,0000 z. ; Bendigo, 34,0000 z. ;
Castlemaine, 600 oz,; while the increases are: Beech-
worth, 18,4000 z. ; Maryboro’, 14,0000 z. ; Gippsland,
13,0000 z. ; Ararat, 46000 z. The Mining Registrars are
only completely in touch with company mining, the
statistics of individual and private effort not being avail-
able to them. The gross amount of their error is indi-
cated by the Mint returns concerning Vic. gold :
Amount minted. Amount “ estimated.”
1893 .. 693,319 671,126
’94 ~ 739,496 673,679
1,432,815 1,344 805
This shows a discrepancy in the “ estimates ” of 88,010 oz.
in two years 1

Addressing a meeting the prospector for a Cool-
gardie syndicate said, the other day, that he was deeply
giieved that the shareholders had bound him down not to
peg-out any claim on less than soz. stone, hut he was
thankful he was now able to do so with the view of selling
to local buyers.
* • ♦ •
“There is not much doing here in mining,’’
writes au old Northern Territory correspondent of Tub
Bulletin. “Most of the old claims have fallen into
Chinese hands. Many Europeans applied months ago for
work on the railway line. It was said that Govt, would
employ all Europeans in place of Chinese after Ist July
of this year ; but, up to now, a number of men who were
trying for work have been put off with‘over age’ (35
years) for Chinamen.”

♦ • » *
The Australian Gold Recovery Company are
acting sensibly at last by erecting a 5-ton cyanide plant,
at (he Austral Otis Company’s ore-treating works, for the
practical treatment of parcels of ore. The practical
management of the Gold Recovery Company now being
in the hands of the well-known American metallurgist,
Mr. Louis Jaunin, jun., recently editor of the Mining and
Engineering Journal of New York, brisk operations will
probably result, providing the validity of the company’s
patent be upheld.*
The accompanying portrait represents the
countenance of Mr. B. Horwitz, the principal
representative of the New York Equitable, who
claims to be the boss life-assurance canvasser of
Australia. People who know Mr. Horwitz, and
have narrowly escaped being insured by him, all
describe him as the most persuasive individual in
his profession. He is a man who does business on
a huge scale, and lays for <£lo,ooo and .£20,000
proposals. On a recent visit to Adelaide he took
the biggest insurance ever effected on a single life
in that province, and most persons who are
insured for very large amounts owe the circum-
stance to the same bland and pressing gentle
Mr. B. HORWITZ.
man. When Dunn, the Wealth of Nations man,
returned to Sydney, Mr. Horwitz gently took
possession of him in the street, led him into
the Equitable office, and took his life there and
then to the extent of 10,000. The much-adver-
tised prospector was a good advertisement for the
man who captured him, and nearly every agent
in Sydney now claims to be the person who
insured Dunn. Mr. Horwitz is an Australian by
birth, a man of no particular age, an amateur con-
juror and thought-reader, and a man of many mis-
cellaneous accomplishments. Also, he is a living
proof of The Bulletin’s theory that fat is stored-
up energy, and that the race in this world is
mo.-tly to the stout.
Honesty in a patent-medicine man may be
anomalous, yet it exists. How frequently does
the physic-vendor extol his bolus as an infallible
cure for all human ills ; how seldom does he
frankly admit that if misapplied his remedies will
fail? Yet there are these exceptions and Mr.
John Francis Taylor, senr., is one of them. Five
years ago this gentleman, while conducting a
chemist’s business in an English midland town,
had his attention drawn by an old gamekeeper to
a remedy he had discovered for piles. Mr. Tay-
lor tested the simple recipe, found its results
marvellous, and finally registered it under the
name of the Keeper pill. After the trouble of
obtaining the natural ingredients in sufficiently
largo quantities had been overcome, Mr. Taylor
launched himself in London and founded his
present large business with very satisfactory
results. Nor, when prosperity set in, was the old
gamekeeper forgotten. Later, when it was found
that haemorrhoids were often complicated with
liver troubles, the company perfected the Keeper
Liver Pill, the success of which has also been
phenomenal. Lastly, the Keeper Health Pill
completes a trio of remedies which, while they
will unfailingly do all they profess, are not to be
regarded as universal panaceas, nor as specifics in
any direction beyond the sphere for which they
were designed.*

320 O-^IIMIIEXjS
320 CAMELS
GREAT SHIPMENT OF
CAMELS!
JUST ARRIVED.
Now on View near Perth.
Pack & Biding Camels of the host Breed,
KHARAN,
From
BELUCHISTAN, and
AFGHANISTAN.
Most reliable for carrying and travelling purposes,
unsurpassed by any hitherto imported from India
—real beauties.
These camels are now at Lime Cliff Paddock,
about 2J miles from Subiaco and 3 miles from
Claremont, where they will remain for three
weeks to undergo the necessary quarantine regu-
lations.
They are undergoing one month’s quarantine,
during which period they will be fed on good,
strengthening food, and no expense will be spared.
These are not Calcutta camels—or, rather, those
that are known as such —but the real Hill breed ;
accustomed to feed on bushes and grass identical
with those of Western Australia; as only used by
the Army Commissariat Department of India,
consequently they can be thoroughly recom-
mended. They are directly shipped from Kurra-
chee, the nearest seaport to Kharan, Beluchistan
and Afghanistan.
The s.s. Claverley, a first-class steamer, giving
comfortable accommodation to 400 camels, was
chartered for the special purpose of bringing from
Kurrachee these 320 camels. All these camels are
in good condition, as we have taken great trouble,
no expense having been spared by Messrs. D. and
J. Fowler on our behalf.
Diggers and Prospectors are
invited to deal at once.
All telegrams and letters should be addressed
to meat “Criterion Hotel, Perth,” until further
notice.
For prices and full particulars address—
Caff. 0. DOUGLAS SAUNDERS,
Manager;
SULTAN RAZ MAHOMED,
7 Victoria Buildings, Coolgardie, W.A.
Bankers: Union Bank, Coolgardie.
N.B. —As these camels are from a cool part of
the East, they will by the time they leave quaran-
tine be in magnificent condition. I insert this as
Indian camels are considered useless by some of
the judges in Coolgardie. This may apply to the
Calcutta camels, but cannot be taken as applying
to Kharan camels.

[27l] CHARLES PINHEY, Registrar-General.
OF FORECLOSURE UNDER REAL PROPERTY ACT.
In the matter of the application of the Metropolitan Mutual Permanent Building and Investment
Association (Limited), mortgagees under memoranda of mortgages from Emma Smith Sprod, Nos.
57,998 and 62,187, and dated respectively, 23rd May, 1882, and 27th September, 1882, over the
whole of the land comprised in Certificate of Title, vol. 586, folio 150.
WHEREAS the abovenamed mortgagees have made application for an order for foreclosure of the
mortgages above referred to, accompanied by such evidence of default for six months and other facts,
as required by section 113 of the Real Property Act : Notice is hereby given, pursuant to the said Act,
that the land comprised within the said mortgages, as hereunder particularised, is offered and will
remain open for sale until 22nd February, 1895, which date has been appointed as the time upon or
after which I, the Registrar-General, will issue to the applicant an order for foreclosure, which will
exclude all claim by the abovenamed mortgagor, and of any person claiming through or under her,
unless in the interval a sufficient amount has been realised by the sale of such land to satisfy the prin-
cipal and interest moneys due, and all costs and expenses occasioned by such proceedings, and interest
up to the time of such sale.
A plan and further particulars may be obtained by application at the Land Titles Office, Elizabeth-
street, Sydney.
Dated at Sydney this 11th day of January, 1895, at the Registrar-General’s Department.
[273] CHARLES PINHEY, Registrar-General.

Dear Bulletin,— Your “Sporting Notions
idviie punters “ not to forget that Carbine, as a three-
year-old, could not win the Australian Cup.” It hap-
pened that Carbine had then to meet Lochiel, at a differ-
ence of lib. only in weights, the famous Maoriland horse
having just previously given Carbine 61bs. and a beating
in their places for the Newmarket Handicap. Moreover,
Carbine was not “cherry ripe” at the time. He had
been training in sweaters before the Fleinington meeting,
and was then running into form. Two days after the
Australian Cup he beat Abercorn for the Champion
Stakes, another two days later he was fit enough to beat
Lochiel by a head, at a difference of libs., in the Loch
Plate. Anon he beat everything at Randwick Autumn
meeting, where he won his first Sydney Cup, with 9st. up.
Had Carbine been specially prepared for the Australian
Cup, aforesaid, he would probably have defeated even
Lochiel. Anyway, his failure on that occasion is not a
sound argument against The Harvester’s chance for next
Australian Cup, seeing that there is no flying Lochiel at
3st. 71bs. in the handicap.—Form.

Petronel is the only son of the great Musket
now at stud in England. His lee is 100 guineas. _ Here in
Australia we have many of the breed, and sires like
Enfilade (full brother to Nordenfeldt) stand for some 20
guineas.

Lord “ Bill ” Beresford is to have a share in
Paris during the “galloping-machine’s" English tour.
Indian experience teaches him that Australian horses can
hold their own.

The Brisbane racing - boats went home from
Sydney without their crack 18-footer, the Victoria, which
has been bought by Doran, the owner of the 16-footer,
Sophia. The Victoria, which brought the 18-foot champion-
ship with her from Brisbane, on Anniversary Day came
second, after a splendid race, to the Ariel, the latest and
best of her class in Sydney.

Dear Bulletin,— Daily papers to the contrary
notwithstanding, Saturday last w if not the hardest blow
for the past 40 years in Sydney harbor. About 17 years
ago the sailing races at Balmain Regatta were not finished
on Prince of Wales’, birthday owing to the wind dying
away, so w* re sailed! off on the following Saturday. It
blew a terrific gnleJtrpm the S.S.W., and of some 23 boats
which started, 23 and 24 open boats class, hut
two could go the Sow and Pigs—the Lottie and the
Young Jivn/r Such men as George Ellis and Gardiner ran
lor shelW; one to Chowder and the other to Watson’s,
and most of the boats either carried their sticks away or
were capsized. Last Saturday was not a patch on that
day, and I have no doubt Ellis, still in active service,
would bear me out.—W.

Joe Goddard is off to England. He has deferred
his visit too long. He was a comparatively old man when
he appeared in public, and every year tells against him.
Still a match between Joe and Slavin, another “ has-
been,” would even now be worth seeing.

A wrestling-matoh between the undefeated
Afghan and Billy Uru, the herculean Maori footballer,
fast-bowler, and wrestler, should prove a great draw.
Since his sixteenth year the Maori has never met an
opponent who had a ghost of a show with him.
The success of the new Oannalin remedies
for piles is most remarkable, large quantities
being sent to all parts of the colonies from the
depot at 257 George-street, Sydney.
Referring to the Mount Jukes gold-dis-
covery, the Strahan correspondent of the Zeehan
Herald writes :—“ J. Harris, a well-known pros-
pector, arrived here from Mount Jukes with some
splendid samples ofstone'all carrying good gold.
The new find is situated upon the western slope of
Mt. Jukes, and about six or seven miles from Mt.
Lyell. The parties have been prospecting there
for some time, and have at last struck what has
evidently shed the gold found in the creeks round
about. Several pieces—in fact, all they had with
them—are now on view, and have caused no end
of excitement here. The reef is from 7ft. to Bft.
wide, carrying good gold throughout. The pros-
pectors have secured two 10-acre reward claims
and three adjoining leases, and have already had
many applications for interests, but it is their in-
tention to, if possible, place the whole thing before
the public, and let them see for themselves what
it is. The fortunate parties are: John Harris,
John Benassi, W. Wilson, and W. Castles.”

Dear Bulletin, —“ Heretic ” misconceives
the new hedonism, which says nothing at all
about man s relation to the universe—the wheel.
What it deals with is the man-fly’s relation to the
other flies who go round with him. That is a per-
fectly legitimate basis for the construction of a
system of morals. The universe doesn’t care
whether or no “ Heretic ” gets three meals a day,
but he probably thinks three meals desirable all
the same. Hedonism leaves the universe out of
the question ; ta-kes it for granted ; and simply
says, things being so-and-so, the best course of
action for humanity is such-and-such. Isn’t that
rational?—B.B.
Mr. Otto Fischer, President of Sydney
German Club, in celebrating the 24th anniversary
of the German Empire at Fern Bay, lavishly
eulogised blood-and-iron Bismarck for his great-
ness as a man. Not as a man, certainly, but as a
machine should Bismarck be called great. Hairy
old Parkes describes him more correctly in one of
his inspired sonnets:
Yet, 0 how little all which men call great
w Has blessed men’s homes or formed a happy State 1
In what faint letters on the scroll of fame
Will be inscribed the world-resounding name 1
How far from those who hold the highest place—
The benefactors of the human race ! ”
The tendency of a nation to grow like its ideals
should restrain citizens from setting up as types
of greatness for a free people men whose leading
trait is the despotic.
The N.S. Wales Government Printer issues
(in the reign of a “ retrenching ” Government) a
bulky, expensive, and lavishly-dismal volume
with the words, “Report of the Executive Com-
missioner for N.S.W. to the World’s Columbian
Exposition, Chicago, 1893,” thundering in an
awful blast of big print over the title-page. As
an example of how the public money goes, and a
beautiful instance of the at-last-bebaubled “Exe-
cutive Commissioner’s” craving after notoriety,
the volume possesses a certain interest, but other-
wise it is a dreary wilderness of print and illus-
trations. It contains nearly 100 pictures, showing
different parts of the Exhibition, views of Chicago,
and other things which excite a feeble interest in
those people who get their big volume gratis, but
which nobody else cares about; likewise 671 pages
of print, and an immense multitude of plans and ele-
vations of all manner of things. The printed matter
deals with such solemn subjects as “Prehistoric
Footprints in Nevada,” “ Rules and Regulations
for the Supply of Compressed Air Power from
Shafting,” “Folk-lore of China,” “Pueblo
Indians,” a prayer by Cardinal Gibbons, and
other things equally irrelevant. Why the N.S.W.
tax-payer should be called upon to pay for prayers
by Cardinal Gibbons and for the diffusion of
knowledge about Chinese lies passes comprehen-
sion.

John Thompson, the suddenly-deceased

In early days, ere Hobart Domain was
fenced along by the cattle-jetties, much terror was
often caused by cattle which, when landed, broke
away and rushed through the city. An old resi-
dent relates that he and two friends were bathing
one morning near the jetties when several beasts
stampeded and charged the bathers, who, while
perfectly nude, had to seek shelter up a tree. In
their excitement they did not notice that someone
else had gone up the same tree till they heard a
voice from above : “Good morning, gentlemen.
An undress levee, I presume, we’d call this.” It
was Gov. Denison, who had got out of harm’s way
when the cattle “ ran amuck.”
Dear Bulletin, —When Civil Service
reform comes along let us hope that the salaries
of will be fixed on a more common-
sense scale than now. Surely these men with nice
easy billets, no brain-work, and no responsibility,
are not entitled to a higher remuneration than the
average tradesman ; indeed, considering that
theirs is a life-job, without broken time, the wages
should really be lower. Good carpenters, masons,
and bricklayers, making uncertainly from .£70 to
£100 a year, may well sigh for the post of an
official flunkey, at £130 per annum certain, with
a good chance of eventually rising to, say, £275.
This scandal exists all over Australasia. In
Maoriland, I knew a messenger who was the land-
lord of the Minister in charge of the department,
and in many Australian departments messengers
grow wealthy by lending small sums to impecu-
nious officials “ till the end of the month.”—Timi
Piripi.

Sixty-five years have gone since the stone
was hewn by a convict-gang for building Darling-
hurst Gaol, Sydney. The quarry where the con-
victs worked was in Woolloomoqloo, at the pre-
sent crossing of Palmer-street with William-st.,
and thence came all the stone for the first gaol-
buildings and wall. The buildings have long ago
been pulled down, and new ones built of Pyrmont
stone. But the old wall has yet a part standing,
and the ugly signs upon it are still as clear as
when the convict cut them to record his output,
for untiring industry meant for him indulgence in
tea and sugar. The contractor for the wall had a
dispute with Govt, when the work was ended, but
the difference was met by giving him a life-
pension of £2 a week and the right to live in a
d veiling of his own inside the gaol wall. So he
built himself a rubble hut near the present black-
smith’s shop, and, having whitewashed it, he
moved in with a bunk, a chair and table, and for
several years lived there, a strange, useless exis-
tence. Whence and whither he came and
went no one seems to know, but warder
M’Master (who has just been pensioned off, and
used to be a sentry at Darlinghurst from ’41 to ’48)
describes him as “ a little wee old man with a wee
-little black dog;” wearing, among other things,
an immense cabbage-tree hat and a pint-and-a
half black bottle. Every morning, about half-
past 10, he came out of his hut, stole silently past
the sentry, and through the gaol gates to the
nearest hotel to get his bottle filled with rum;
then he returned as silently as he had gone, went
into his hut and stayed there all day by himself.

The “Voice from the Hush.”
Editor Bulletin, — Whatever may be the
history of this poem now more or leas
authoritatively attributed to Mowbray Morris
—it is perhaps worth while, just “for the
of God and of Gwendoline,” to note some
internal evidence pointing, apparently, to Gordon
as the author, after all. In spite of the inferior
literary merit of the “Voice,” as compared with
the acknowledged works of the centaur-poet, there
exist between them correspondences of thought
and expression difficult to explain away.
“ Ve Wearie Wayfarer ” and the “ Voice’’open
on the same scene, with the difference that, in the
former, “Two-thirds of my journey, at least, is
done,” and, in the latter, “I’ve half the day
before me yet, and most of my journey done.”
Then the reflections of the reposing traveller,
however extended and diversified in “Ye W. W.,”
are almost its counterpart in sporting reminiscence,
and are like nothing else outside Gordon. The
gratuitous justification of sport, occurring twice in
“Ye W. W.” and once in “ Hippodromania,”
appears also in the “Voice.” Now, observe the
correspondence in a rather singular thought. In
the “ Sick Stockrider ” we have :
For good undone and gifts mis spent and resolutions
vain,
’Tis somewhat late to trouble. This I know—
I should live the same life over if I had to live again ;
And the chances are I go where most men go.
In the “ Voice ”
Of the seed I’ve sown in pleasure,
The harvest I'm reaping in pain ;
Could I put my life a few years back,
Would I live that life again ?
Would I? Of course, I would 1 &o.
Such recurn noe of thought, more or less
obvious, may be found in every stanza of the
“Voice.” Nor are verbal parallelisms lacking.
In the “ Sick Stockrider ” we have :
The hardest day was never then too hard.
In the “ Voice ” :
The hardest work is never too hard,
Or (sic) the longest day too long.
Again, in “ Cui Bono,” we have :—•
On this earth so rough we know quite enough,
And, I sometimes fancy, a little too much.
In the “ Voice ” :
We knew too much, but not quite enough.
The expressive mannerism “here and there”
appears at least twice in Gordon, and, in similar
context, we have it in the “ Voice.” The word
“little,” in its adverbial application, is very freely
used by Gordon, and in the “Voice” we have
“ 1 here’s little enough of shade to be got.” Such
verbal parallelisms abound —trivial, taken one by
one, but, collectively, helping to thicken other
proofs.
In reading Gordon, we are struck by the many
familiar allusions to his pipe. Three instances are
found in “ Ye W. W.,”two in “Hippodromania,”
one in the “Sick Stockrider,” and one in the
“ Exile’s Farewell.” The author of the “Voice”
repeats the reference, and exactly in Gordon’s
manner.
Of the 47 poems which compose the collection of
Gordon’s acknowledged works, 30, at least, end
with a picture or suggestion of death ; and in 11
of these, the thought, morbidly introverted, ap-
pears as a personal forecast, a desirable exchange
of the intolerable for the unknown. This, by the
way, is observable in “ Argemone ” (which ap-
peared in The Bulletin, of Oct. 20), and is one
of the many internal evidences of that poem’s
authenticity. The same prospective refuge occurs
to the thought-sick and futureless poet of the
“ Voice.”
Again, Gordon is not, in the strict sense of the
term, an Australian poet. He is Anglo-Austra-
lian. His best poetry, of course, is cosmopolitan
in scope and age-long in currency; but, entering
the sphere of tangible things, his early culture
asserts itself, and his imagination, by very neces-
sity of its heroic tendency and retrospective bias,
dwells on military glory, on classic and mediaeval
tradition, on the excellence of the Anglo-Saxon
race and the Norman blood. His only purely
Australian poems are the “ Hippodromania ”
series, the “Sick Stockrider,” the “Dedication,”
“From the Wreck,” “Wolf and Hound,” “Gone,”
and the “ Basket of Flowers.” Others, such as
“Ye W. W.,” the beautiful “Whisperings in
Wattle-Boughs,” &0., are merely English jewels
in Australian setting. To him “Snows are rife in
December, and sheaves are in August yet.” The
sound of cattle-bells recalls “ The chimes of
sweet St. Mary’s, on far English ground.” In
the only Australian fytte of “Ye W, W.” it
occurs to the moralising poet that “In Exeter
Hall the saints may chide.” He cannot get
beyond the English exile. “ How We Beat the
Favorite” is as grossly English as J. B. Patter-
son. Gordon’s English reminiscences are free and
felicitous; his Australian reminiscences are, by
comparison, forced and perfunctory.
So with the author of the “ Voice.” Instead of
the parrot’s screech, this soliloquist also seems to
hear the twang of the horn, &o. His semi-military
and sporting reminiscences of England are also
glowing and unconstrained beside the woxlen
pseudo-memories of Australian life.
This suggests another agreement in impress of
authorship. Gordon is a “ gentleman ” —a sterling
man, to be sure, but a “gentleman,” nevertheless.
The far-reaching curse of caste is upon him—just
like Original Sin upon the present writer. His
sympathies are with the Oavaliers, with Sir John
and His Grace, with baron and squire, and knight
of the shire. His only Australian hero is the aris-
tocratic, but hideously incapable, Burke. There
is nothing democratic in Gordon’s fellowship,
though ; to his great credit, he rarely sinks into
that most pitiable of all vulgarities, namely, the
direct assumption of a soap-bubble distinction
offensive alike to gods and men.
Now, the same old-world instinct of indefinable
superiority to Brown, Jones, and Robinson is a
pervading element in the “Voice,” rendered con-
spicuous by the intense subjectivity of the poem—
a subjectivity which may count as another coin-
cidence in tendency with Gordon’s acknowledged
works. This ingrained sentiment mars the
verisimilitude of the Australian memories in the
“Voice” by dictating such names as Jack
Villiers, Byng (broke in the Derby of ’5B), and
Harry Lepel—presumably occupants of a station
hut. And here we are reminded of Frank Mostyn
and Carisbrooke (bai Jove!) in a suspiciously
similar reminiscence of the “Sick Stockrider.”
The author of the “ Voice ” is among: the lads,
but not of them; he is not only a reduced gentle-
man, but accepts his fate exactly in Gordon’s
spirit. Indeed, “Banjo’s” “Voice from the
Town ” fails in consistency as a sequel —or, rather,
companion-poem—to the “Voice,’’only in that it
is Australian instead of Anglo-Australian, and
isocratic instead of class-suggestive.
Let it be clearly understood that no disparage-
ment of Gordon is implied in these last para-
graphs. Brave, truthful, chivalrous, magnani-
mous to a fault, he voices unspoken thought in
every generous heart, Australian and foreign; and
what higher tribute can be claimed or conceded
than the great and growing esteem of readers
who, by the especial grace of Providence, are
neither Englishmen nor “gentlemen ! ”
To conclude, the Poems and the “ Voice ” are
alike distinguished by the tyranny of memory, by
the absence of humor and of rancour, and by a
forced and inadequate stoicism, thinly veiling
sensitive self-pity. So that, without pronouncing
positively on the origin of the “ Voice,” it may be
taken as falling fairly within Gordon’s narrow
and exceptional range, and as being more closely
allied to that poet’s works by mental and verbal
parallelism than to any other collection in the
language—requiring, indeed, nothing but a Scrip-
tural allusion and a scrap of Latin or Greek to
make the family resemblance perfect. And the
crude, faulty, unlicked diction—alternating with
good strong Gordon-like lines—may be a blemish
pertaining to half-finished and rejected work.
Then it only remains to condone the absence of
secondary rhymes. y
Shepparton, Vic. Tom Ooluns.
[Note.—The Bulletin prints this letter because it is
interesting, because the incidental criticism of Gordon is
good, and because the view that Gordon wrote “ A Voice
from the Bush ” is still held by many readers on similar
evidence to that adduced—apart from the fact that the
verses appear in every published edition of Gordon’s
works. The question of authorship was, however,
definitely set at rest some years ago. In Howlett-Ross’s
memoir of Gordon (p, 86 et seq.) the matter is fully dis-
cussed. After quotation of some of the parallelisms
instanced by “Tom Collins,” it is stated that the poem
has been emphatically claimed by Mr. Mowbray Morris
(now of London Times) in a U tter written to Major Fer-
guson, of the S.A. Bide Brigade. Mr. Morris says:
“ Certainly the verses are mine. I remember both the
time and place where I wrote them—lying on my back in
a cave at Robe in the autumn of 1871. ... I was not
aware that there was any sim larity, unconscious or other-
wise, to any verses of Gordon’s. I have two volumes of
his verse by me, and I cannot detect any conscious [? un-
conscious] plagiarisms. Certainly there were none con-
sciously committed. Mine they are, every line and every
word, and they have no business among the writings of
anyone else.” Apparently the verses were first published
anonymously in Adelaide Advertiser ; but the editor,
when applied to, had forgotten the name of the author;
and Marcus Clarke doubtfully inserted them In his edition
of Gordon when sent him by an unknown correspondent.
There is evidence that Mr. Morris claimed them about the
time of publication ; Gordon never claimed them ; and
their inclusion with his worics is apparently due only to
the fact that style and matter are similar in some points
to his. That similarity, however, is easily accounted for.
“ The Sick Stockrider,” the poem of Gordon’s which
“A Voise from the Bush” most resembles, was pub-
lished in “Bush Ballads and Galloping Rhymes” in
1870, and had previously appeared in print. Doubt-
less Mowbray Morris read it, and writing in 1871—
next year, with the verses fresh in his memory—he
plagiarised unconsciously or otherwise. Probably Gor-
don’s verse, and the likeness of Gordon’s experience
to his own, suggested to Morris to try his hand. There is
little force in “Tom Collins’” corroborative instances.
Every bush poet puts his pipe—and his horse, and a
woman—into his poetrjn They are the only three reliable
“properties” he has—in imagination; for among the
wattles of real life the woman and the horse are generally
unreliable. So the pipe is closest to his heart of all. And
death—varied by suggestions of heaven and hell-seems
to be the natural climax of all Australian verse. Lawson
almost invariably kills bis heroes; “The Banjo” sends
old Pardon careering round celestial race-courses; and
even Daley walks Larry over the Milky Way. Anyhow,
in the absence of rebutting evidence, and with such an
explanation of the coincidences available, Morris’s direct
statement must be taken as conclusive; and “A Voice
from the Bush ” should henceforth disappear from Gor-
don’s published poems.—Ed. B.]
A Modern Miracle.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You have heard of Balaam’s donkey, and the woe-
ful words he spoke
When the prophet tried coercion on that dispu-
tatious moke,
And though the age of miracles long since departed
hence,
They occasionally happen in this age of common
sense.
And the one I am expounding— which my words
are strictly true—
Is sufficiently astounding to knock aged Balaam
“ blue.”
It occurred to Jerry Jowler—whose case I think
was sad,
Though I’m not at all disputing that his character
was bad—
Still, about his antecedents I have no desire to
speak.
Simply, then-he got in trouble and was brought
before the beak ;
And the “screw” who apprehended him accused
hun then and there
Of creating a disturbance in a public thoroughfare,
And with using dreadful language—which the
bobby said he swore
His immediate intention was to wallow in some
gore!
And Jerry chewed a straw, as if he didn’t care a
rap,
And wiped, unostentatiously, his nose upon his
cap.
He was not intoxicated, though abstracted was his
gaze,
And the beak looked grim and muttered (sotto
voce) —“ Seven days.”
Now, I’ve known this Jerry Jowler from his boy-
hood’s happy hours,
Without once dreaming he possessed weird, super-
human powers ;
And yet he was the agent of a miracle first-class,
For undoubtedly poor Jerry was as dumb as
Balaam’s ass !
And when a chap swears freely who’s been deaf
and dumb from birth,
I maintain that deeds miraculous still happen on
this earth.
What! another explanation? No, it’s really (oo
absurd,
And you can’t be such a sceptic as to doubt a
bobby’s word.
P.

Sold: A Retrospect.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was in Maoriland in the sixties—that is, in
the days when the diggers dreamed not of prohi-
bition, and when one was much more certain than
now to get in a mining town a dram of decent
stuff incapable of blowing the cork out.
Plainly, there was something astir. Everybody
had on his best. A new barmaid had arrived
(there is no hope for the wretches who would
rob the diggers of that comfort) at Widow
Brown’s pub. In twos, threes, or sixes, the whols
place dropped in, four or five times an hour, to
have “another gaze.”
She was the sole topic of conversation. “ Ain’t
she clean !” said one. “ That wur a slashing feed
we had to-day !” said another. “ Best tucker ever
I struck !” added a third, with a furtive glance
from beneath the rim of a hat that seemed
intended as a coyer for his face.
By the end of the week, the new-comer was no
longer a stranger. Everybody know her. She
was a woman of over 30, marvellously preserved.
She had never been married, though—well, she
had been queen of many gold-rushes.
The man who has spent a quarter-of-a-eentury in
the Australian provinces has seen ten civilisations,
anyway. There is ground for belief that later
civilisations have degenerated.
The woman of to-day is not the cook of the past.
There is an air of cindered chops about the present
hapless creature. To-day too many matrons want
their daughters to play “the planner.” Too
many mothers wash the clothes, and get the lan-
guid girls to bring them in. (Thus the man passing
by, the clothes-line is deceived.) Therefore, our
civilisation is comparable to a needle which has
been too finely sharpened and too much reduced
at the sides. This reduction has been visible
throughout Australia for twenty years past.
Now, the consequences of this fatal relapse of
woman are manifold and terrible. The bad cook
is only a slow murderess.
But there was grit in our barmaid. She was
none of these. She could produce a meal worthy
of Man— and digging breeds men. She was tidy
to a hair. She was trim as a snug vessel. And —
she was handsome.
Of course the whole district became infatuated
with her. Diggers go that way. They sit in the
solitude of their huts and dream of the ideal in
the guise of the handiest flesh-and-blood substi-
tute. In the night it is their companion. In this
way do single miners love a million times—it is
their polygamy. They love with a distant, wor-
shipful love—“ the desire of the moth for the
star.”
They are pleased if she visits their claim and
watches the water flying from the sluicing-nozzle.
They show her the rainbow under the jet. They
bring her nuggets. And if she speaks to them, be
it only a “ Good-day !” they fancy that their
affection is making headway, and rejoice in every
fibre of their being.
Out of her admirers the charmer reduced the
cluster to two, and between these the contest was
terrible. They hung about the place like unto
dogs chained. She ran against one, or the other,
or both, each minute. She spake to them singly,
or in a pair, forty odd times an hour. It was evi-
dent to the boys that something was “ looming up
ahead.”
The aspirants were named Louis and Charles,
both Frenchmen and over 40. The worst of a
Frenchman is that France hangs like a plumb-bob
before his nose, and everything has to be regarded
across France. But the regard here was not of
conceit—it was fury.
One night there was a rumor that Charles had
struck a “ pile ”in his claim. People began to
bet whether our ideal would be influenced by the
find. It was freely asserted that night would see
it decided.
I was hot and restless during the evening, and I
went down to the sea-beach, two miles distant. I
sat on a log and smoked and let the sweet hum of
the Pacific eat its way into my ears. This con-
tent of reverie surpasses the pleasure of books,
and is the nearest approach to the pleasure of
gratified love which a mind undistorted can dis-
cover on this crude planet.
Suddenly I saw a figure streaming out towards
the sea. It was Louis.
“ Don’t be a fool! ” I said, as I locked my arm
in his and led him away. He had been rejected.
Additional confirmation was granted when the
pair were seen walking out for a stroll on the
morrow.
They were married. 1 didn’t see the wedding
(a most informal transaction) as I had all my
work cut out to guard Louis. May I never again
take such a contract!
Every wedding involves a question of senti-
ment. One morning the husband went to cut
wood. He returned suddenly and took a silent
peep into his apartments. Then he stole off on
tip-toe for his gun. That is the other end of the
sentiment. When he returned the room was
empty ; the birds had fled.
And because he couldn’t find anybody to shoot
he pined away and grew silly. When I left, the
abandoned one had made his claim over to a
stranger.
From that day the story is a blank. The
denouement is unknown. But wherever she is I
swear the scones in the cupboard will be capital.
As said before, she was a good cook. Some have
thought that cookery is a form of virtue. They
ewed. Virtue is a form of cookery.
POLYPE.
Mr. Geo. Collingridge is publishing, at
Hornsby Junction, N.S.W., an illustrated journal
called Progekss, devoted to recording facts about
early Australian history. The number which has
reached The Bulletin contains some good ex-
amples of Mr. Collingndge’s skill as wood-en-
graver.
Chief Justice Way, when he did his last
soulful grovel to the departing Kintore, entreated
that magnate to have his speeches published in
book-form, because the people of S. A. were pining
for such a record. The Bulletin trusts that some
other Governor will fall in with this suggestion.
Nothing is so calculated to keep a Governor
humble, and to prevent him saying too much
about the silken bonds, and to remind him that
he isn’t nearly so interesting as a cricket-match
or a football-scrum, as the sight of his volume of
speeches in the threepenny-basket outside a
second-hand bookshop. A good many Governors
who have made fools of themselves in days gone
by might have died amid general respect if they
had had an unsaleable volume of speeches to keep
them in a meek and contrite mood.
A REMINISCENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Many years ago, in Central Queensland, there was
a drought so savage and long-continued that it
caused the abandonment of whole districts.
Without doubt other causes tended to this end.
Kangaroos, and—let us say, banks. But the fact
remains, that the interior of Western Australia is
not, at the present day, a more utter desert than
was one large area which I and some others
were, at that time, unfortunately for us, forced to
traverse.
There was water on it, true; but the
eaten by worms before they died, swarmed over
the land, and left the mark of their poisonous
teeth, worse than a goat’s, on every vestige of
decent herbage.
For something grew upon their trail that was
neither grass nor weed, only quite unwholesome.
They even did not thrive on it, for they were
shrunken and nerveless. An ancient sheep-dog that
we picked up, more than half-dying, and restored
to life, used to bark at them ; and, after a feeble
run, they would totter and fall over.
The edges of the scrubs were lined with the
bones of the lesser marsupial vermin, and there
was no man in the land but ourselves. Gaunt
corner-posts of deserted huts rose here and there.
Stations of the order of that day, for it was long
before the dandy modern times, stood tenantless,
waiting for any ad venturous swagman to enter and
assume the rdle of squatter. But none was there,
and of those who were with me, some went north
to the Gulf, and bad whisky soon killed them ;
some went west, and the niggers knocked
them on the head ; some came south, and died of
over-feeding. I believe that I am the only sur-
vivor of that trip.
It was not as if it were a desert-land, wherein no
footstep had fallen before. On the contrary. On
every side were signs of former habitation, rotting
fast into decay, for there was naught to make a
bush-fire to destroy them ; nothing but barren
clurpps of scrub, beyond which no fire would
travel. Nobody came there, except Tottering
kangaroos. There were no natives. There was
actually nothing living from morn to night but
dying marsupials of all types.
There were lagoons, truly, but in hard, barren
ridges ;. and some few colts, who had seemingly
been left behind, sustained existence by plunging
their heads deep down under water and grasping
at the green, wet weed below, coming up to breathe
with long tussocks between their jaws. This weed,
I suppose, with pickings from the scrub, kept
them alive.
We fed our own horses on what flour we had,
and tied them up at night so that they should not
wander on the poisonous trail left by the dying
marsupials.
Always there was a brazen sky, an awful sun,
and never a drop of moisture. Always there were
the remains of what man had once built, but never
a sign of his presence. You could have left a pile
of sovereigns in one of the shaking huts, and re-
turned in a month to find them there, untouched.
When the rain eventually came, and grass
sprang up, a long, trailing vine, something
like an exaggerated sweet-potato, grew also, and
covered the earth. This soon disappeared, and
the country resumed its normal state. But one
good thing had been effected by the drought:
the marsupials, big and little, had disappeared;
and, when they again appeared, the advance of
time had made them of some commercial value,
which was in itself sufficient to keep their numbers
n check.

Napoleon told General Leclerc he must go
to San Domingo. Leclerc : “But, sire, I cannot
leave-my unprotected sister.” Nap.: “I will
have her well married, this very day.” Shortly
afterwards General Davoust came in. “ Sire, I
have to ask your consent to my marriage with
.” “Oh, yes, I know, Mdlle. Leclerc.”
“No, sire! ” But he had to marry her, and they
were very happy.
Five days before the news of R. L.
Stevenson’s death reached England, the editor of
the magnificent new Edinburgh edition of his
works sailed for Samoa with the two first volumes.
Stevenson was keenly interested in the progress of
the work, which was crowning evidence of the
esteem accorded to his talent; but its first fruits
reached him only in time to be laid as memorials
upon his grave.
Melb. Herald publishes the alleged
“prison experiences” of a member of the staff,
who was requested (he says) by the manager to
“ do a fortnight or so in Melbourne gaol, and get
a bit of solitary confinement thrown in.” Then the
obvious romancer goes on torelatehowhe promptly
got himself “remanded,” and so forth, all to
oblige the manager aforesaid. He doesn’t state
the nature of the offence which was to earn a bit
of “ solitary ” in a sentence of 14 days, nor explain
why he was remanded.
A proposal is mooted in London for the
construction of a big hotel for honeymoon couples
only. The rooms are to be specially decorated to
suit the complexion of every possible variety of
bride. The guests won’t stare at the brides, for
they willallbeinthesame situation, and the waiters
will be discreet men specially warranted not to
snigger at their endearments. Newly-married
couples may hug each other all over the house,
there being no cynical bachelors or old, hard-faced
wives about to object; and they may coo till far
into the night without the family in the next suite
of apartments objecting. No attendant will come
into a room without knocking several times and
dropping various heavy articles. Also, all these
things will be charged in the bill, on the assump-
tion that a newly-married couple is too blissfully
dazed to argue about overcharges.
